Shuf'ah in a Fasid Sale: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Building and Right to Rescind
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shuf'ah in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why shuf'ah does not apply in a fasid sale until the right to rescind is lost, and contrast the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when the buyer builds.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, pre-emption waits until the right to rescind is lost; Abu Hanifa says building ends that right, the Sahibayn say it does not.

We have already learned that shuf'ah is not established in a fasid sale because the seller retains the right to rescind. But what if the buyer builds on the property? Does that extinguish the right to rescind, thus opening the door for pre-emption? The Hanafi school splits on this issue. In this lesson, we examine the debate between Abu Hanifa and his two distinguished students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
State the baseline rule
Begin by recalling the general principle: shuf'ah does not apply in a fasid sale because the seller has the right to rescind (haqq al-faskh). The buyer's ownership is provisional.
This is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams.
2
Identify the trigger event
What could cause the right to rescind to be lost? The passage highlights building (al-bina') by the buyer with the seller's permission as a possible extinguisher.
Building is a form of investment that makes rescission harmful to the buyer.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that building ends the right to rescind, thus enabling shuf'ah. His proof: the right to rescind is a right of the Lawgiver (haqq al-shar'), not a personal right; the buyer's need and permission override it.
The buyer built with permission, so the seller cannot demolish.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that building does not extinguish the right to rescind. Their proof: the seller's right to reclaim is stronger than the shafi'i's right; the seller can demolish the building and reclaim the property.
For them, the seller's right is a personal right that cannot be lost by the buyer's unilateral action.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical consequence: if the buyer builds, Abu Hanifa says shuf'ah applies; the Sahibayn say it does not. Later Hanafi fatwa often prefers Abu Hanifa's view, but the text presents both without tarjih.
Also note the distinction between initial and subsequent defect in the last part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بيعت فاسدا إلخ: أي ولا تجب الشفعة أيضا في دار بيعت بيعا فاسدا ما لم يسقط حق الفسخ بالبناء؛ لأن هذا البيع مستحق الفسخ. وإذا سقط الفسخ بأن بنى المشتري فيها انقطع حق البائع في الاسترداد، وجبت الشفعة فيها عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما (أبي يوسف ومحمد) لا ينقطع حقه في الاسترداد فلا تجب الشفعة فيها، ويسترد وينقض ما بناه المشتري؛ لأن حق البائع في الاسترداد أكد من حق الشفيع، وحقه لا يبطل، وله نقض البناء، والبائع أولى. وله (أبي حنيفة) أن حق الاسترداد في البيع الفاسد حق الشارع، والبناء حق المشتري، وحقه مقدم لحاجته، والمشتري بنى بتسليط البائع، فلا يكون له نقضه. وهذا إذا وقع الفساد في البيع الفاسد في الابتداء، وأما إذا طرأ الفساد، فإنه يبقى حق الشفعة كما لو اشترى ذمي من ذمي دارا بخمر، فلم يتقابضا حتى أسلما، أو أسلم أحدهما، أو قبض الدار، أو لم يقبض الخمر، فإن البيع يفسد، وللشفيع الشفعة.
Or was sold in a fasid manner: that is, shuf'ah is also not established in a house sold in a fasid sale until the right to rescind is lost by construction. Because this sale is subject to rescission. When the right to rescind is lost, e.g., by the buyer building on it, the seller's right to reclaim is cut off, and shuf'ah is established according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his right to reclaim is not cut off, so shuf'ah is not established; he may reclaim and demolish what the buyer built, because the seller's right to reclaim is stronger than the shafi'i's right, and his right is not invalidated; he may demolish the building, and the seller is more entitled. Abu Hanifa's argument: the right to reclaim in a fasid sale is a right of the Lawgiver, while the building is the buyer's right, and his right is given precedence due to his need; and the buyer built with the seller's permission, so the seller may not demolish it. This applies if the defect occurs initially in the fasid sale. If the defect arises later, the right of shuf'ah remains, e.g., if a dhimmi buys a house from another dhimmi for wine, and they do not take possession until both embrace Islam, or one of them, or he took possession of the house but not the wine, the sale becomes fasid, and the shafi'i has shuf'ah.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute about when pre-emption applies in a fasid (voidable) sale. The sale is valid but defective, so the seller has the right to rescind. While that right exists, the buyer's ownership is provisional, so pre-emption is not established. Abu Hanifa holds that if the buyer builds on the property, the seller's right to rescind is lost (because removing the building would harm the buyer), and then pre-emption applies. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller's right remains, and he can reclaim the property and demolish the building; thus pre-emption never arises because ownership is not fully transferred. The passage also distinguishes between initial fasid sales (where the defect exists from the start) and subsequent defect (e.g., a valid sale becomes fasid due to change in circumstances); in the latter, pre-emption rights persist. The example of wine purchase illustrates this: if the contract is initially valid (wine is property for dhimmis) but becomes fasid upon one or both embracing Islam, the buyer has taken possession, so pre-emption applies.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is valid in essence but defective due to a missing condition, such as sale of wine to a Muslim; subject to rescission.
حق الفسخ (haqq al-faskh): The right to rescind the contract; in a fasid sale, the seller has this right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when does pre-emption apply after a fasid sale of a house?
A) Immediately at the time of sale.
B) When the buyer builds on the property.
C) Only if the seller rescinds the sale.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah in a fasid sale is conditional on the loss of the right to rescind, and the Hanafi imams differ on whether building effects that loss.

Revival of Pre-emption after Surrender: Iqaalah vs Judicial Rescission
Ruling on pre-emption when the shafi' surrenders his right, then property returned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where pre-emption revives and does not revive after surrender, and apply the Hanafi majority position.

Core Idea
A surrendered pre-emption right revives if the property is re-transferred in a way that constitutes a new sale for the shafi'.

Once a shafi' gives up his right, is it gone forever? The answer depends on how the property returns to the seller. This lesson examines the difference between a pure rescission (faskh) and a transaction that is legally a new sale (iqaalah), and how each affects pre-emption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial surrender
The shafi' has surrendered his pre-emption right after the property was sold to a buyer. This is the starting point.
Surrender can be explicit or implicit.
2
Distinguish types of return
The property is returned to the seller. Classify the return: is it a judicial rescission (e.g., by qadi for defect or option of seeing) or a non-judicial mutual rescission (iqaalah or return for defect without qadi)?
Judicial rescission is pure faskh; non-judicial is akin to a new sale.
3
Apply the Hanafi majority rule
If the return is by iqaalah or defect return without qadi, it is considered a new sale in respect of the shafi'. Therefore, pre-emption revives.
The parties' intention to rescind does not override third-party rights.
4
Note Zufar's objection
Zufar argued that surrender is final and iqaalah is mere rescission, so no revival. Understand his reasoning based on the parties' intent.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the rejoinder
The majority responds that iqaalah is a cause of ownership like a sale, and the parties have no authority to nullify the shafi's right. Hence, a new sale triggers a new pre-emption right.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو سلمت شفعته إلخ أي ولا تجب الشفعة في دار اشتريت وسلم الشفيع الشفعة، ثم ردت الدار إلى البائع بخيار الرؤية أو شرط كيفما كان أو ردت بخيار عيب بقضاء القاضي؛ لأنه فسخ من كل وجه، فلا يمكن أن يجعل عقدا جديدا، فعاد إلى قديم ملك البائع، ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يكون الفسخ قبل القبض أو بعده. وتجب لو ردت إلخ يعني تجب الشفعة إن ردها المشتري بعيب بغير قضاء، أو تقايلا البيع، وقال زفر: لا تجب؛ لأن شفعته بطلت بالتسليم، والرد بالعيب بغير قضاء إقالة، والإقالة فسخ لقصدهما ذلك، والعبرة بقصد العاقدين، قلنا: الإقالة سبب للملك بتراضيهما كالبيع، غير أنهما قصدا الفسخ، فيصح فيما لا يتضمن إبطال حق الغير؛ لأن لهما ولاية على أنفسهما، ولا ولاية لهما على غيرهما، فيكون بيعا جديدا في حق الشفيع، فيتجدد له به حق الشفعة. قال الشارح: ومراده بالرد بالعيب الرد بعد القبض.
Or surrendered his pre-emption: that is, shuf'ah is not established in a house that was bought and the shafi' surrendered his pre-emption, then the house was returned to the seller due to option of seeing, or option of condition, whatever it may be, or was returned due to option of defect by a qadi's ruling, because it is a rescission in every sense, so it cannot be made a new contract; it reverts to the old ownership of the seller, and there is no difference whether the rescission is before or after possession. And it is established if it is returned: meaning, shuf'ah becomes established if the buyer returns the property due to a defect without a qadi's ruling, or if they mutually rescind (iqaalah). Zufar said: it is not established because his pre-emption was invalidated by surrender, and the return due to defect without a ruling is an iqaalah, and iqaalah is a rescission because of their intention, and consideration is given to the intention of the contracting parties. We said: iqaalah is a cause of ownership by mutual consent like a sale, but they intended rescission, so it is valid as long as it does not entail annulling the right of a third party; because they have authority over themselves, but no authority over others. So it is a new sale in respect of the shafi', and thus his right of pre-emption is renewed. The commentator said: his meaning of return due to defect is return after possession.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage covers scenarios where a shafi' has surrendered his pre-emption right but later the property is returned to the seller. If the return is via a judicial rescission (e.g., khiyar al-'ayb by qadi's ruling) or through options like khiyar al-ru'yah, it is considered a pure rescission (faskh), reverting ownership to the seller without a new sale, so pre-emption does not revive. However, if the buyer returns the property due to a defect without a court order (i.e., the parties agree to rescind), or if they mutually rescind (iqaalah), this is considered a new sale in the eyes of the shafi' because it involves a transfer of property with mutual consent. Consequently, the shafi' regains his right of pre-emption. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagreed, arguing that the surrender once given is final and the return is a rescission, not a new sale. The majority answer is that iqaalah is like a sale for the shafi'i's right, as the parties have no authority to nullify his right. Thus, a new sale gives a new chance for pre-emption.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqaalah): Mutual rescission of a sale by the seller and buyer; in Hanafi law, it is considered a new sale in respect of third party rights.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract that reverts ownership to its prior state.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption right of a neighbour or partner to purchase property that has been sold to an outsider, under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a shafi' surrenders his pre-emption right, under which of the following does his right revive according to the Hanafi majority?
A) The buyer returns the property due to a defect after a qadi's ruling.
B) The buyer and seller mutually rescind the sale (iqaalah).
C) The seller exercises his option when the buyer surrenders.
D) The property is returned due to khiyar al-ru'yah.

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption right revives after surrender only if the property is returned through a transaction that constitutes a new sale for the shafi', such as iqaalah or return for defect without a qadi's ruling.

Pre-emption in Voidable Sales and Division Among Partners
Expands the rule on voidable sales and division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which pre-emption is not due in a voidable sale and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding building.

Core Idea
Pre-emption is not due in a voidable sale until building drops the right of revocation; division among partners also blocks pre-emption.

This passage expands on the general rule that pre-emption is not due as long as the right of revocation is not dropped by building, or the property is divided among partners. It then delves into a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about whether building in a voidable sale triggers pre-emption, and briefly touches on why division prevents pre-emption. By understanding these scenarios, you will be able to apply the ruling correctly in transactions where the sale is defective or ownership is shared.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Read the initial statement: pre-emption is not due as long as the right of revocation is not dropped by building, or the property is divided among partners, or the pre-emptor surrenders and the property is returned by court order. Focus on the first two conditions.
The third condition (surrender and return) will be covered in the next lesson.
2
Understand the Voidable Sale Scenario
The passage states that in a voidable sale (bay' fasid), pre-emption is not due unless the right of revocation is dropped by the buyer building on the property. This is because the sale is subject to rescission.
A voidable sale is one that is defective in its subject matter or price but not fundamentally void (batil).
3
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that once the buyer builds, the seller's right of recovery is cut off, and pre-emption becomes wajib. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that the seller can still recover and demolish the building, so pre-emption is not due. Understand the reasoning: Abu Hanifa prioritizes the buyer's need (istihsan), while the Sahibayn prioritize the seller's right (qiyas).
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Apply the Rule to Division Among Partners
The passage explicitly states that division (qismah) among partners does not trigger pre-emption for the neighbor because it is an act of separation (ifraz), not an absolute exchange. The right of pre-emption is only for absolute exchanges.
This applies to co-owners dividing jointly owned property; the neighboring owner cannot claim pre-emption.
5
Distinguish Initial vs Supervening Voidability
The passage differentiates: if the sale is initially valid but later becomes void (e.g., a wine sale where the parties convert to Islam before possession), pre-emption remains. This is because the defect is not present at the time of contract.
This nuance shows that pre-emption is evaluated at the moment of the sale, not after supervening events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة
ما يجب فيه الشفعة وما لا يجب
ما لم يسقط حق الفسخ بالبناء، أو قسمت بين الشركاء، أو سلمت شفعته، ثم ردت بخيار رؤية أو شرط أو عيب بقضاء، وتجب لو ردّت بلا قضاء، أو تقايلا.
الدار إلى البائع
الدار إلى البائع بخيار عيب أي البائع والمشتري البيع ملکه، - بالاتفاق، وإنما اختلف في أنه هل دخل في ملك المشتري أو لم يدخل والشفعة تثبت بإعراض البائع عن . فإنه إذا ادعى أنه باع داره من زيد، فجحده زيد وجبت الشفعة؛ لاعترافه بخروجه عن ملكه. (من الفتح والتكملة) أو بيعت فاسدا إلخ: أي ولا تجب الشفعة أيضا في دار بيعت بيعا فاسدا ما لم يسقط حق الفسخ بالبناء؛ لأن هذا البيع مستحق الفسخ وإذا سقط الفسخ بأن بني المشتري فيها انقطع حق البائع في الاسترداد وجبت الشفعة فيها عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا ينقطع حقه في الاسترداد فلا تجب الشفعة فيها ويسترد وينقض ما بناه المشتري؛ لأن حق البائع في الاسترداد أكد من حق الشفيع، وحقه لا يبطل، وله نقض البناء، والبائع أولى، وله: أن حق الاسترداد في البيع الفاسد حق الشارع، والبناء حق المشتري، وحقه مقدم لحاجته، والمشتري بنى بتسليط البائع، فلا يكون له نقضه. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٢/٢] وهذا إذا وقع الفساد في البيع الفاسد في الابتداء، وأما إذا طرأ الفساد، فإنه يبقى حق الشفعة كما لو اشترى ذمي من ذمي دارا بخمر، فلم يتقابضا حتى أسلما، أو أسلم أحدهما، أو قبض الدار، أو لم يقبض الخمر، فإن البيع يفسد ، وللشفيع الشفعة. (الفتح وغيره)
أو قسمت بين الشركاء إلخ أي ولا تجب الشفعة لجارهم بالقسمة في دار قسمت بين الشركاء؛ لأن القسمة فيها معنى الإفراز، ولهذا يجري فيها الجبر والشفعة لم تشرع إلا في المبادلة المطلقة وهي المبادلة من كل وجه. رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٢/٢] أو سلمت شفعته إلخ أي ولا تجب الشفعة في دار اشتريت وسلم الشفيع الشفعة، ثم ردت الدار إلى البائع بخيار الرؤية أو شرط كيفما كان أو ردت بخيار عيب بقضاء القاضي؛ لأنه فسخ من كل وجه، فلا يمكن أن يجعل عقدا جديدا، فعاد إلى قديم ملك البائع، ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يكون الفسخ قبل القبض أو بعده رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٢/٢]
وتجب لو ردت إلخ يعني تجب الشفعة إن ردها المشتري بعيب بغير قضاء، أو تقايلا البيع، وقال زفر: لا تجب؛ لأن شفعته بطلت بالتسليم، والرد بالعيب بغير قضاء إقالة، والإقالة فسخ لقصدهما ذلك، والعبرة بقصد العاقدين، قلنا: الإقالة سبب للملك بتراضيهما كالبيع . غير أنهما قصدا الفسخ، فيصح فيما لا يتضمن إبطال حق الغير؛ لأن لهما ولاية على أنفسهما، ولا ولاية لهما على غيرهما، فيكون بيعا جديدا في حق الشفيع، فيتجدد له به حق الشفعة. قال الشارح: ومراده بالرد بالعيب الرد بعد القبض [تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٧٦/٨]
Book of Pre-emption. What necessitates pre-emption and what does not. As long as the right of revocation is not dropped by building, or division among partners, or he surrendered his pre-emption; then it is returned due to option of sight, condition, or defect by court decree—and it is obligatory if returned without court decree or by mutual rescission. The house to the seller. The house to the seller due to option of defect: i.e., the seller and the buyer, the sale—his property by agreement; they only differed whether it entered the buyer's ownership or not. And pre-emption is established by the seller's relinquishment of his property: if he claims that he sold his house to Zayd, and Zayd denies it, then pre-emption is obligatory due to his admission that it left his ownership. (From al-Fath and al-Takmilah) Or if it is sold in a voidable sale: i.e., pre-emption is not obligatory in a house sold in a voidable sale as long as the right of revocation is not dropped by building; because this sale is subject to revocation. If the revocation is dropped by the buyer building on it, the seller's right of recovery is cut off, and pre-emption becomes obligatory according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, his right of recovery is not cut off, so pre-emption is not obligatory; he recovers and demolishes what the buyer built, because the seller's right of recovery is stronger than the pre-emptor's right. His right is not nullified, so he may demolish the building; the seller takes precedence. And according to him (Abu Hanifa): the right of recovery in a voidable sale is a divine right, while the building is the buyer's right, and his right takes precedence due to his need. The buyer built with the seller's permission, so he cannot demolish it. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/342) This is when the defect occurs in the voidable sale from the beginning; but if the defect supervenes, then the right of pre-emption remains, as if a dhimmi buys from a dhimmi a house with wine, and they do not take possession until they both become Muslim, or one becomes Muslim, or he takes possession of the house, he does not take possession of the wine—the sale becomes void, and the pre-emptor has pre-emption. (al-Fath and others) Or if divided among partners: i.e., pre-emption is not obligatory for their neighbor in a house divided among partners, because division involves separation; hence compulsion applies in it. And pre-emption is only legislated for absolute exchange, which is exchange in every respect. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/342) Or if he surrendered his pre-emption: i.e., pre-emption is not obligatory in a house purchased and the pre-emptor surrendered his pre-emption, then the house is returned to the seller due to option of sight or condition in any manner, or returned due to option of defect by court decree; because it is a rescission in every respect, so it cannot be considered a new contract; it returns to the seller's former ownership. There is no difference in this whether the rescission occurs before or after possession. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/342) And it is obligatory if returned: i.e., pre-emption is obligatory if the buyer returns it due to a defect without court decree, or if they mutually rescind the sale. Zufar said: it is not obligatory, because his pre-emption was invalidated by surrender; and returning due to defect without court decree is a rescission, and rescission is a dissolution due to their intention; regard is given to the intention of the contracting parties. We say: rescission is a cause of ownership by mutual consent like a sale; except that they intended dissolution, so it is valid in what does not involve nullifying a third party's right, because they have authority over themselves but not over others. So it is a new sale regarding the pre-emptor, and thus the right of pre-emption renews for him. The commentator said: his meaning by 'return due to defect' is the return after possession. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/276)
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the chapter on when pre-emption (shuf'ah) is obligatory and when it is not. The author first states the general rule: pre-emption is not due as long as the right of revocation (faskh) is not dropped by building, or the property is divided among partners, or the pre-emptor surrenders his right and then the property is returned due to option of sight, condition, or defect by court order. However, if the property is returned without court order or by mutual rescission (iqaalah), then pre-emption becomes obligatory. The commentary then elaborates each condition. First, it clarifies that if the seller claims to have sold his house but the buyer denies, the seller's admission that the property left his ownership establishes the pre-emptor's right. Next, the passage delves into a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a voidable sale (bay' fasid). If a sale is voidable, pre-emption is not due unless the buyer builds on the property, thereby dropping the right of revocation. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, once the buyer builds, the seller's right of recovery is cut off, and pre-emption becomes obligatory. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller's right remains intact, so he can recover the property and demolish the building, and pre-emption is not due. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the right of recovery in a voidable sale is a divine right, while the buyer's building is his own right, and his need takes precedence. The companions argue that the seller's right is stronger than the pre-emptor's. The passage then differentiates between initial voidability and supervening voidability: if the sale was initially valid but later becomes void (e.g., a wine sale by dhimmis who later convert), pre-emption remains. The second condition: division among partners (qismah) does not trigger pre-emption because it is an exchange in the sense of separation, not absolute exchange. The third condition: if the pre-emptor surrenders his right and then the property is returned to the seller due to options (defect, sight, condition) by court order, pre-emption does not revive because the rescission is complete as a dissolution. However, if the return occurs without court order—i.e., the buyer voluntarily returns the property due to a defect—or by mutual rescission, then the rescission is considered a new sale from the perspective of the pre-emptor, so his right revives. This position is preferred over that of Imam Zufar, who argued that the pre-emptor's right is permanently lost upon surrender. The passage ends with a note by the commentator that 'return due to defect' refers to return after possession.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property that has been sold to a third party, by paying the price, to prevent harm from non-partner ownership.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A voidable sale: a contract that is defective in its subject matter or price (e.g., selling wine) but can be rectified; the contract is not automatically void but is subject to rescission by one party.
إقالة (iqaalah): Mutual rescission: both parties agree to cancel the contract. It is considered a new sale from the perspective of third party rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does pre-emption become obligatory in a voidable sale?
A) Immediately upon the sale contract.
B) When the buyer builds on the property.
C) When the seller admits the sale.
D) When the property is divided among partners.

Key Takeaway
In a voidable sale, pre-emption is not due until the buyer builds, which cuts off revocation per Abu Hanifa; division among partners also blocks pre-emption.

Revival of Pre-emption After Surrender and Return
Expands the rule on surrender and return of property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where surrender of pre-emption is final and where it revives upon return of the property, and apply the ruling to scenarios of return due to options or mutual rescission.

Core Idea
Surrender of pre-emption is final only if the property returns by court order; voluntary return or mutual rescission creates a new sale, reviving the right.

This passage covers the third condition from the general rule: surrender of pre-emption by the pre-emptor, followed by a return of the property to the seller due to options (sight, condition, defect). The key distinction is whether the return is by court decree or without court decree (voluntary). If voluntary, the return is considered a new sale, reviving the pre-emptor's right. Imam Zufar disagreed, but the Hanafi school holds that the right revives. By the end of this lesson, you will understand when revival occurs and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the general rule: pre-emption is not due if the pre-emptor surrenders his right, and then the property is returned to the seller due to option of sight, condition, or defect by court decree. If returned without court decree or by mutual rescission, pre-emption is due.
This sets up the distinction.
2
Understand Why Court-Ordered Return Does Not Revive
When a court orders the return, it is a pure rescission (faskh) from every respect. It does not create a new sale; the property reverts to the seller's ownership as if the sale never happened. Therefore, the pre-emptor's surrender remains final.
Court-ordered return is involuntary from the buyer's perspective; it is not a new transaction.
3
Compare with Voluntary Return or Mutual Rescission
If the buyer returns the property voluntarily (e.g., after discovering a defect, without going to court) or if both parties mutually rescind (iqaalah), this is considered a new sale (bay' jadid) from the perspective of third parties. The pre-emptor gains a new right because a new exchange of property occurs.
The test is whether the return is a voluntary exchange or a forced annulment.
4
Analyze Zufar's Objection
Imam Zufar argued that once the pre-emptor surrenders, his right is permanently lost. The voluntary return is merely a rescission (faskh), not a new sale, because the parties intended to cancel the contract. The Hanafi response: the parties' intention only affects themselves; they cannot nullify a third party's right by their agreement. Therefore, their rescission is a new sale regarding the pre-emptor.
This is a key usul point: intention is limited to the contracting parties; third party rights are independent.
5
Apply to a Concrete Example
Consider a pre-emptor who surrenders his right. Then the buyer returns the house to the seller due to a hidden defect, without going to court. The pre-emptor can now claim pre-emption again because the voluntary return is a new sale.
The passage specifies that 'return due to defect' refers to return after possession (qabd).

الدار إلى البائع

(الفتح وغيره)

Book of Pre-emption. What necessitates pre-emption and what does not. As long as the right of revocation is not dropped by building, or division among partners, or he surrendered his pre-emption; then it is returned due to option of sight, condition, or defect by court decree—and it is obligatory if returned without court decree or by mutual rescission. The house to the seller. The house to the seller due to option of defect: i.e., the seller and the buyer, the sale—his property by agreement; they only differed whether it entered the buyer's ownership or not. And pre-emption is established by the seller's relinquishment of his property: if he claims that he sold his house to Zayd, and Zayd denies it, then pre-emption is obligatory due to his admission that it left his ownership. (From al-Fath and al-Takmilah) Or if it is sold in a voidable sale: i.e., pre-emption is not obligatory in a house sold in a voidable sale as long as the right of revocation is not dropped by building; because this sale is subject to revocation. If the revocation is dropped by the buyer building on it, the seller's right of recovery is cut off, and pre-emption becomes obligatory according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, his right of recovery is not cut off, so pre-emption is not obligatory; he recovers and demolishes what the buyer built, because the seller's right of recovery is stronger than the pre-emptor's right. His right is not nullified, so he may demolish the building; the seller takes precedence. And according to him (Abu Hanifa): the right of recovery in a voidable sale is a divine right, while the building is the buyer's right, and his right takes precedence due to his need. The buyer built with the seller's permission, so he cannot demolish it. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/342) This is when the defect occurs in the voidable sale from the beginning; but if the defect supervenes, then the right of pre-emption remains, as if a dhimmi buys from a dhimmi a house with wine, and they do not take possession until they both become Muslim, or one becomes Muslim, or he takes possession of the house, he does not take possession of the wine—the sale becomes void, and the pre-emptor has pre-emption. (al-Fath and others) Or if divided among partners: i.e., pre-emption is not obligatory for their neighbor in a house divided among partners, because division involves separation; hence compulsion applies in it. And pre-emption is only legislated for absolute exchange, which is exchange in every respect. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/342) Or if he surrendered his pre-emption: i.e., pre-emption is not obligatory in a house purchased and the pre-emptor surrendered his pre-emption, then the house is returned to the seller due to option of sight or condition in any manner, or returned due to option of defect by court decree; because it is a rescission in every respect, so it cannot be considered a new contract; it returns to the seller's former ownership. There is no difference in this whether the rescission occurs before or after possession. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/342) And it is obligatory if returned: i.e., pre-emption is obligatory if the buyer returns it due to a defect without court decree, or if they mutually rescind the sale. Zufar said: it is not obligatory, because his pre-emption was invalidated by surrender; and returning due to defect without court decree is a rescission, and rescission is a dissolution due to their intention; regard is given to the intention of the contracting parties. We say: rescission is a cause of ownership by mutual consent like a sale; except that they intended dissolution, so it is valid in what does not involve nullifying a third party's right, because they have authority over themselves but not over others. So it is a new sale regarding the pre-emptor, and thus the right of pre-emption renews for him. The commentator said: his meaning by 'return due to defect' is the return after possession. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/276)
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the chapter on when pre-emption (shuf'ah) is obligatory and when it is not. The author first states the general rule: pre-emption is not due as long as the right of revocation (faskh) is not dropped by building, or the property is divided among partners, or the pre-emptor surrenders his right and then the property is returned due to option of sight, condition, or defect by court order. However, if the property is returned without court order or by mutual rescission (iqaalah), then pre-emption becomes obligatory. The commentary then elaborates each condition. First, it clarifies that if the seller claims to have sold his house but the buyer denies, the seller's admission that the property left his ownership establishes the pre-emptor's right. Next, the passage delves into a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a voidable sale (bay' fasid). If a sale is voidable, pre-emption is not due unless the buyer builds on the property, thereby dropping the right of revocation. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, once the buyer builds, the seller's right of recovery is cut off, and pre-emption becomes obligatory. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller's right remains intact, so he can recover the property and demolish the building, and pre-emption is not due. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the right of recovery in a voidable sale is a divine right, while the buyer's building is his own right, and his need takes precedence. The companions argue that the seller's right is stronger than the pre-emptor's. The passage then differentiates between initial voidability and supervening voidability: if the sale was initially valid but later becomes void (e.g., a wine sale by dhimmis who later convert), pre-emption remains. The second condition: division among partners (qismah) does not trigger pre-emption because it is an exchange in the sense of separation, not absolute exchange. The third condition: if the pre-emptor surrenders his right and then the property is returned to the seller due to options (defect, sight, condition) by court order, pre-emption does not revive because the rescission is complete as a dissolution. However, if the return occurs without court order—i.e., the buyer voluntarily returns the property due to a defect—or by mutual rescission, then the rescission is considered a new sale from the perspective of the pre-emptor, so his right revives. This position is preferred over that of Imam Zufar, who argued that the pre-emptor's right is permanently lost upon surrender. The passage ends with a note by the commentator that 'return due to defect' refers to return after possession.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqaalah): Mutual rescission: both parties agree to cancel the contract. It is considered a new sale from the perspective of third party rights.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission: annulment of a contract, returning the property to its previous owner. In court-ordered faskh, it is a pure rescission without creating a new sale.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect: the buyer's right to return the goods if a defect is discovered, subject to conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition does pre-emption NOT revive after surrender?
A) When the buyer returns the property voluntarily due to a defect.
B) When the buyer returns the property due to a defect by court order.
C) When the buyer and seller mutually rescind the contract.
D) When the buyer returns the property before taking possession.

Key Takeaway
Surrender of pre-emption is not final if the property returns voluntarily; the right revives because the return is a new sale for the pre-emptor.

Invalidation by Abandoning the Two Demands
Ruling with proof on invalidation of shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two demands that preserve the right of pre-emption, identify the conditions under which abandoning them invalidates shuf'ah, and distinguish between valid and invalid acts of surrender.

Core Idea
Abandoning the immediate demand or the demand of notification while able invalidates pre-emption.

This passage explains that the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) is not permanent; it can be lost by the pre-emptor's own actions. The two demands—immediate (talab al-muwathabah) and notification (talab al-taqrir)—are essential to preserve the right. Abandoning either or both when one is able and aware constitutes i'rad (turning away), which nullifies the claim. The commentator also clarifies a subtle point: it is the abandonment of the demand itself, not mere failure to testify, that causes invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Demands
Recognise talab al-muwathabah (immediate demand upon learning of the sale) and talab al-taqrir (demand of notification, e.g., before a judge or witnesses). Both are required to maintain shuf'ah.
The first demand must be made without delay; the second confirms the right formally.
2
Understand Conditions for Invalidation
Abandonment only invalidates if the pre-emptor has knowledge of the sale, has the ability to demand (not prevented, not in prayer), and freely chooses not to act.
If prevented or in prayer, abandonment does not count.
3
Distinguish I'rad from Testimony
The commentator corrects a misconception: it is not the absence of testimony that invalidates, but the intentional turning away from the demand. Testimony is not a condition; the demand itself is.
This refines the wording in al-Hidayah.
4
Apply the Principle to Surrender and Conditional Waiver
Pre-emption cannot be surrendered before the right accrues. After accrual, voluntary dropping (isqat) is valid, even if conditional. For example, saying 'I surrender if you buy for yourself' is only effective if the buyer indeed purchases for himself.
Surrendering to the buyer's agent is valid.
5
Note Other Invalidators
Besides abandonment, shuf'ah is also invalidated by a settlement for compensation (with obligation to return), or by the death of the pre-emptor (not the buyer).
These are listed in the matn but not detailed here.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يبطل به الشفعة وتبطل بترك طلب المواثبة أو التقرير، وبالصلح من الشفعة على عوض، وعليه رده، وبموت الشفيع، لا المشتري، تبطل بموته باب ما يبطل به الشفعة لما كان بطلان الشيء يقتضي سابقة وجوده ذكر ما تبطل به الشفعة بعد ذكر ما تثبت به الشفعة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۷/۸] وتبطل إلخ أي تبطل الشفعة بترك أحد الطلبين أو كليهما حين علم مع القدرة عليه بأن لم يمنعه أحد، ولم يكن في الصلاة؛ لأنها تبطل بالإعراض وترك أحدهما أو كليهما مع القدرة إعراض على ما تقدم، والأصل في هذا الباب أن تسليم الشفعة قبل البيع لا يصح، وأن من ثبت له الحق إذا أسقطه بعد ثبوته له سقط علم بثبوته له أو لم يعلم، وتعبير المؤلف بترك الطلب أولى من تعبير صاحب "الهداية" بترك الإشهاد؛ لأنه يرد على صاحب "الهداية" أن الإشهاد ليس بشرط، وترك ما ليس شرطا في شيء لا يبطله، وفي "المحيط " : لو سلم الشفعة للوكيل صح وسقطت ويصح تعليق الإسقاط بشرط، ولو قال: سلمت لك إن اشتريت لنفسك لم تبطل إذا كان اشتراها لغيره تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۷/۸] أو التقرير بأن لم يشهد على أحد المتبايعين ولا عند العقار (طائي)
Chapter on what invalidates pre-emption. And it is invalidated by abandoning the demand of promptness or the demand of notification, and by settlement from pre-emption for compensation, and upon him is its return, and by the death of the pre-emptor, not the buyer, it is invalidated by his death. Chapter on what invalidates pre-emption: Since the invalidation of something necessitates its prior existence, he mentions what invalidates pre-emption after mentioning what establishes pre-emption (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:277). 'And it is invalidated etc.' means pre-emption is invalidated by abandoning one or both demands when he knows and is able, provided no one prevents him and he is not in prayer; because it is invalidated by turning away (i'rad), and abandoning one or both while able is turning away as previously. The principle in this chapter is that surrendering pre-emption before sale is not valid, and that whoever has a right, if he drops it after its establishment, it drops whether he knows of its establishment or not. The author's expression of 'abandoning the demand' is better than the author of al-Hidayah's expression 'abandoning testimony' because it refutes al-Hidayah that testimony is not a condition, and abandoning something that is not a condition does not invalidate it. In al-Muhit: If he surrenders pre-emption to the agent, it is valid and drops, and suspending the dropping on a condition is valid. If he says, 'I surrender to you if you buy for yourself', it is not invalidated if he bought for another (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:277). Or the demand of notification is that he does not testify before either of the two contracting parties or at the property (Ta'i).
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on what nullifies pre-emption (shuf'ah). The author follows the principle that invalidation presupposes prior existence, so after discussing how pre-emption is established, he now explains what destroys it. The matni (base text) lists several invalidators; this section focuses on abandoning the two demands. The first demand is 'talab al-muwathabah' – the immediate demand made as soon as one learns of the sale, which must be done without delay and usually in the presence of witnesses or at the property. The second is 'talab al-taqrir' – the demand of notification, which means confirming the right before a judge or witnesses. Abandoning either or both while able to do so constitutes i'rad (turning away) and invalidates the right. The commentator clarifies that this applies when the pre-emptor has knowledge and ability, is not in prayer, and is not prevented. He also notes a dispute with al-Hidayah: abandonment of testimony is not what invalidates, because testimony is not a condition; rather, it is the abandonment of the demand itself. The passage also includes the rule that surrendering pre-emption before the sale is invalid, but after the right accrues, voluntary dropping (isqat) is effective. The commentary from al-Muhit adds that if the pre-emptor surrenders to the buyer's agent, it is valid; however, a conditional surrender like 'I surrender if you buy for yourself' does not invalidate if the agent buys for another. The section ends with a definition of talab al-taqrir: failing to testify before either party or at the property.

Key Terms
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathabah): The immediate demand of pre-emption made as soon as the pre-emptor learns of the sale, before leaving the place or engaging in other business.
طلب التقرير (talab al-taqrir): The demand of notification, i.e., confirming the right before a judge or witnesses to establish the claim.
الإعراض (al-i'rad): Turning away from or abandoning the right, which is a manifestation of intent to drop the claim.
الإشهاد (al-ishhad): Testimony or bringing witnesses; the commentator notes this is not a condition for shuf'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what invalidates shuf'ah?
A) Failing to testify before witnesses
B) Abandoning the immediate demand or the demand of notification while able
C) Selling the property to another person
D) The death of the buyer

Key Takeaway
The right of pre-emption is lost if the pre-emptor abandons either the immediate demand or the demand of notification while knowledgeable and able, because such abandonment constitutes i'rad (turning away).

Invalidation of Pre-emption by Settlement on Compensation
Explains that settling pre-emption for a consideration invalidates the right and mandates return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on settling pre-emption for compensation and explain why it is invalid according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Settling pre-emption for compensation is invalid because the right is only to acquire ownership, not an established property.

This passage addresses a key invalidator of pre-emption: the pre-emptor accepting compensation to drop his right. The Hanafi position is clear: such a settlement is void, and any compensation taken must be returned because it is akin to a bribe. Understanding the distinction between a right to acquire ownership (haqq al-tamlik) and actual ownership is essential to grasping this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the buyer settling with the pre-emptor for a compensation to drop the right of pre-emption. The ruling: pre-emption is invalidated, and the pre-emptor must return the compensation.
The compensation is considered a bribe (rishwah).
2
Understand the 'illa (legal reason)
The pre-emptor's right is not an ownership right in the property; it is merely a right to acquire ownership (haqq al-tamlik). Therefore, taking compensation for it is not permissible because it is not a property right that can be sold or exchanged.
This is based on the principle that only established property rights can be exchanged for compensation.
3
Contrast with analogous cases
In cases of qisas (retaliation), marriage ownership, and dropping slavery, the ownership is established in the property (mutlaq fi al-mahal). Hence, compensation may be taken for dropping those rights. This contrast clarifies the unique nature of shuf'ah.
The ownership of qisas is considered a right established in the person of the perpetrator, whereas shuf'ah is only a potential right to purchase.
4
Determine the consequence
Since the compensation was taken for a non-property right, it must be returned because it is analogous to a bribe. The pre-emptor forfeits his right of pre-emption by this settlement.
The ruling ensures that pre-emption remains a protective right for co-owners, not a tradable commodity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالصلح إلخ : يعني تبطل الشفعة إذا صالح المشتري الشفيع على عوض؛ لأنه أخذ العوض عن حق ليس بمال فسقط حقه وعليه رده يعني على الشفيع رد العوض؛ لأن حق الشفيع ليس بتقرر في المحل، وإنما هو مجرد حق التملك، فلا يجوز أخذ العوض، بخلاف الاعتياض عن القصاص وملك النكاح وإسقاط الرق؛ لأن ملكه في هذه الأشياء متقرر في المحل. (عيني، فتح) وعليه رده أي على الشفيع رد العوض لأنه رشوة. (عيني)
'And by settlement etc.' means: Pre-emption is invalidated when the buyer settles with the pre-emptor for a compensation; because he took the compensation for a right that is not property, so his right drops, and upon him is its return, meaning upon the pre-emptor to return the compensation; because the pre-emptor's right is not established in the property, it is merely a right of ownership, so taking compensation is not permissible. Unlike taking compensation for qisas, ownership of marriage, and dropping of slavery, because ownership in these things is established in the property. ('Ayni, Fath) 'And upon him is its return' means upon the pre-emptor to return the compensation because it is a bribe. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage explains the second invalidator: settlement (sulh) on a compensation for shuf'ah. When the buyer pays the pre-emptor something to drop his right, the pre-emption is invalidated. The justification is that shuf'ah is not a right of ownership in the land itself; it is merely a right to acquire ownership (haqq al-tamlik). Therefore, taking a monetary compensation for it is not permissible, and if taken, it must be returned. The commentator distinguishes this from cases like qisas (retaliation), marriage ownership, and drop of slavery, where the right is firmly established in the property, so exchange for compensation is valid. The return of the compensation is obligatory because it is akin to a bribe (rishwah). This ruling underscores that shuf'ah is a personal right to purchase, not a transferable property interest.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A settlement or compromise where the pre-emptor agrees to drop his right in exchange for compensation.
حق التملك (haqq al-tamlik): The right to acquire ownership, as opposed to actual ownership of the property itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must the pre-emptor do if he accepts compensation to drop his right of pre-emption?
A) He may keep the compensation because it is a valid settlement
B) He must return the compensation because it is a bribe
C) He may keep the compensation if the buyer agrees
D) He must give half to charity

Key Takeaway
Settling pre-emption for compensation invalidates the right and mandates return of the compensation, as shuf'ah is only a right to acquire ownership, not an established property.

Death of Pre-emptor Invalidates Shuf'ah – A Hanafi–Shafi'i Dispute
Explains that death of pre-emptor invalidates shuf'ah, but buyer's death does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the effect of the pre-emptor's death on shuf'ah, explain the Hanafi proof distinguishing shuf'ah from qisas, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is a mere personal opinion, not a property right; it cannot be inherited.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a key dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) survives the death of the pre-emptor. Understanding this difference requires a careful analysis of the nature of shuf'ah as a legal right and the conditions for its exercise. The Hanafi school holds that shuf'ah is invalidated upon the pre-emptor's death, while Imam al-Shafi'i treats it like other inheritable rights.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: death of the pre-emptor invalidates shuf'ah; it is not inherited. Also note that death of the buyer does not invalidate.
The distinction between pre-emptor and buyer is critical.
2
State the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that shuf'ah is a recognized right like qisas or the right to return a defective item, and thus inheritable.
This analogy (qiyas) is the basis of the dispute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the Hanafi reasoning: shuf'ah is a mere right of ownership (haqq al-tamlik) and a personal opinion (ra'y), not a property right. Therefore, it is not inherited. Also, the condition of continuous ownership of the basis property from sale to taking is not met upon death.
Focus on the distinction from qisas: qisas involves a personal obligation that makes the debtor like a slave, allowing compensation and inheritance.
4
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes shuf'ah to qisas and the right of return due to defect, which are inherited. Understand the basis of his qiyas.
The Shafi'i view considers shuf'ah a substantive right attached to the property.
5
Understand the Hanafi response and tarjih
The Hanafi response refutes the analogy by showing key differences: qisas allows compensation (diyah) and involves a form of ownership over the offender's life, while shuf'ah is merely a personal privilege. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the Hanafi position.
Note the exception: if the pre-emptor dies after a court ruling or the buyer surrenders, the property goes to the heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبموت الشفيع إلخ : يعني بموت الشفيع قبل الأخذ بعد الطلب أو قبله تبطل الشفعة، ولا تورث عنه، ولا تبطل بموت المشتري، وقال الإمام الشافعي: لا تبطل بموت الشفيع أيضا ؛ لأنه حق معتبر كالقصاص، وحق الرد بالعيب. ولنا أنه مجرد حق، وهو حق التملك، وأنه مجرد رأي وهو الصفقة، فلا يورث عنه، بخلاف القصاص؛ لأن من عليه القصاص صار كالمملوك لمن له القصاص، ولهذا جاز له أخذ العوض عنه، وملك العين يبقى بعد الموت، فأمكن إرثه، بخلاف الشفعة؛ لأنها مجرد رأي، ولهذا لا يجوز الاعتياض عنها، ولأن ملك الشفيع فيما يأخذ يشترط أن يكون باقيا من وقت البيع إلى وقت الأخذ بالشفعة، ولم يوجد في حق الميت وقت الأخذ، ولا في حق الوارث وقت البيع، فبطلت؛ لأنها لا تستحق بالملك الحادث بعد البيع، ولا بالزائل بعد الأخذ، وإذا مات الشفيع بعد قضاء القاضي له بالشفعة، أو سلم المشتري الدار له، فهي لورثته يأخذونها. وإنما لا تبطل بموت المشتري؛ لأن المستحق باق، ولم يتغير سبب حقه، وإنما انتقل إلى الورثة كما إذا انتقل إلى غيره -
'And by the death of the pre-emptor etc.' means: By the death of the pre-emptor before taking after the demand or before it, pre-emption is invalidated, and it is not inherited from him, and it is not invalidated by the death of the buyer. Imam al-Shafi'i said: It is not invalidated by the death of the pre-emptor either, because it is a recognized right like qisas and the right of return due to defect. And for us (Hanafis), it is a mere right, and it is a right of ownership, and it is mere opinion (ra'y) and the bargain, so it is not inherited from him, unlike qisas, because the one upon whom qisas is due becomes like a slave to the one who has the right of qisas, and therefore it is permissible to take compensation for it, and ownership of the property remains after death, so inheritance is possible, unlike shuf'ah because it is mere opinion, and therefore taking compensation for it is not permissible. And because the ownership of the pre-emptor in what he acquires requires that it remain from the time of sale to the time of taking by shuf'ah, and it does not exist in the case of the deceased at the time of taking, nor in the case of the heir at the time of sale, so it is invalidated; because it is not established by ownership arising after the sale, nor by ownership ceasing after taking. And if the pre-emptor dies after the judge's ruling for him in shuf'ah, or the buyer surrenders the house to him, then it is for his heirs, they take it. And it is not invalidated by the death of the buyer because the right holder remains, and the cause of his right has not changed, but it is transferred to the heirs just as if it transferred to another.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute regarding the effect of death on the right of shuf'ah. According to the Hanafi school, if the pre-emptor dies, whether before or after making the demand, his right of pre-emption is invalidated and does not pass to his heirs. However, if the buyer dies, the right remains against the buyer's estate and can be enforced against the heirs. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees and holds that death of the pre-emptor does not invalidate shuf'ah, analogizing it to qisas and the right to return a defective item. The Hanafi response is that shuf'ah is merely a right to acquire ownership (haqq al-tamlik), not a property right established in the land. It is a personal privilege based on the pre-emptor's opinion (ra'y) at the time of the bargain. Therefore, it cannot be inherited. The argument distinguishes shuf'ah from qisas where the person subject to punishment is like a slave to the victim, making the right inheritable. A key point is that for shuf'ah to be exercised, the pre-emptor's ownership of the basis property (e.g., the adjacent land) must exist continuously from the sale until the taking. At the moment of the pre-emptor's death, his ownership of the basis property ceases, and the heir did not own it at the time of sale, so the condition is unmet. However, if the pre-emptor dies after a court award or after the buyer voluntarily surrenders the property, then the property belongs to his heirs because the right has been perfected into ownership. The death of the buyer does not affect the claim because the right is against the property, and the buyer's obligation transfers to his heirs.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property sold to a third party, usually by paying the price.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in homicide or injury, where the right is considered a property right that can be transferred or compensated.
حق التملك (haqq al-tamlik): Right to acquire ownership: a legal capacity to become owner upon fulfillment of conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to shuf'ah if the pre-emptor dies before taking the property?
A) The right passes to his heirs
B) The right is invalidated
C) The right is suspended until his heirs are appointed
D) The buyer may keep the property without any claim

Key Takeaway
Death of the pre-emptor invalidates shuf'ah in the Hanafi school because it is a personal opinion, not a property right, except after a court ruling or surrender when ownership is perfected.

Invalidation of Shuf'ah by Pre-emptor's Actions
Rules on invalidation of shuf'ah by pre-emptor's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on which actions of the pre-emptor invalidate the right of shuf'ah and explain why knowledge is irrelevant.

Core Idea
Selling the basis property before court decision invalidates shuf'ah, even if the pre-emptor was unaware.

Shuf'ah (pre-emption) is a right that can be lost if the pre-emptor acts inconsistently with claiming it. This lesson explains how selling, renting, or converting the property that gives the right (e.g., a shared wall or adjacent land) before the court awards shuf'ah can invalidate the claim. The Hanafi school treats these acts as clear evidence of abandonment, and crucially, the pre-emptor's knowledge of the sale that triggered shuf'ah does not matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify invalidating acts
Read the opening sentence: selling the property that is the basis for shuf'ah before the court decision invalidates shuf'ah. Also note that renting, haggling, and converting the property into a mosque, cemetery, or endowment have the same effect.
2
Understand the role of knowledge
The text explicitly says that selling invalidates whether the pre-emptor knew of the purchase or not. This is because the act itself is treated as an explicit surrender (taslim).
Compare with ibra' (releasing a debtor) – also invalidates without knowledge.
3
Recognize exceptions
A sale with an option (khiyar) does not invalidate because the sale is not final. The pre-emptor's shuf'ah remains until the option is exercised.
4
Synthesize the principle
All invalidating actions share a common element: they show the pre-emptor's intention to abandon the right, either by disposing of the property or by acting inconsistently with claiming shuf'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبطل أيضاً وببيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة، ولا شفعة لمن باع أو بيع له، أو ضمن عن البائع، ومن ابتاع أو ابتيع له، فله الشفعة، أي الاستحقاق الدرك لزوال سبب بسبب آخر، فينقضه ويأخذها كما ينقض سائر تصرفاته حتى المسجد ،والمقبرة ولا تباع الدار في دين المشتري؛ لأن حق الشفيع مقدم على حق المشتري، فإن باعها القاضي أو وصيه في دين الميت، فللشفيع أن ينقضه كما لو باعها المشتري في حياته لا يقال: بيع القاضي حكم منه، فكيف ينقض؛ لأنه قضاء منه مخالف للإجماع. تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۸/۸] (فتح) وببيع ما يشفع به إلخ يعني تبطل الشفعة ببيع الدار التي يشفع بها قبل القضاء أي قبل حكم الحاكم بالشفعة؛ الاستحقاق، ولا فرق بين أن يكون عالما وقت بيع داره بشراء المشفوعة، أو لم يكن عالما؛ لأنه لا يختلف في الحالين فصار كالتسليم الصريح، فإنه لا يختلف بين أن يعلم بيعها أو لم يعلم وكذا إبراء الغريم؛ لأن كل ذلك إسقاط، فلا يتوقف على العلم كالطلاق والعتاق. رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٣/٢] ولو باع التي يشفع بها بشرط الخيار لا تبطل شفعته ولو اشتراها الشفيع من المشتري بطلت شفعته؛ لأنه بالإقدام على الشراء أعرض عن وكذا إذا استأجرها الشفيع، أو ساومها بطلت؛ لأنه دليل الإعراض، ولو جعل الشفيع ما يشفع به مسجدا أو مقبرة أو وقفا قبل أن يقضي له بالشفعة، بطلت شفعته ملتقط . ولا شفعة لمن باع إلخ أي لا شفعة للذي باع بالوكالة بأن كان الشفيع وكيلا لبائع الدار المشفوعة؛ لأنه موكل بإتمام البيع، وفي أخذه بالشفعة نقض ما وكل بإتمامه وعند الثلاثة تجب له الشفعة، وكذا لا شفعة لمن والضمير في "له" يرجع إلى "من" يعنى بيع العقار لأجله بأن كان البائع مضاربا والشفيع رب المال فباع الدار، ليس لرب المال الشفعة فيها؛ لأن البيع له، وكذا إذا كان عبدا مأذونا له في التجارة فباع الدار ليس للمولى الشفعة فيها لما ذكرنا رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٣/٢] أو ضمن الدرك إلخ: أي إذا ضمن الشفيع عن البائع الدرك وهو ما يلزم البائع بعد الاستحقاق، فلا شفعة له؛ لأن تمام البيع إنما كان من جهته، فليس له أن ينقض ما تم من جهته تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۷۹/۸] لأنه بضمانه الدرك ضمن له أن تحصل له الدار، وذلك لا يكون إلا بتركه الشفعة وفي أخذه بها إبطال ذلك، وعند الثلاثة: تحب. (عيني) ومن ابتاع إلخ: أي من اشترى أو اشتري لأجله فله الشفعة، أما إذا اشترى بطريق الوكالة وهو شفيع، الشفعة؛ لأنه ليس فيه إبطال شرائه، بل فيه تقرير من جهته؛ لأن الأخذ بالشفعة مثل الأخذ بالشراء، وأما إذا اشتري له فكذلك كالمضارب إذا اشترى عقارا من مال المضاربة، ورب المال شفيعها، كان لرب المال الشفعة، وكذا العبد المأذون إذا اشترى فللمولى الشفعة إذا كان على العبد دين وإن لم يكن عليه دين فلا فائدة بالأخذ؛ لأنه ملكه، ولم يتعلق به حق الغير [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/٢]
It is also invalidated by selling what is the basis for shuf'ah before the court decision on shuf'ah, and there is no shuf'ah for one who sold or for whom a sale was made, or who guaranteed the seller against liability (al-darak), and whoever bought or for whom a purchase was made, he has shuf'ah, i.e., the right of acquisition. [The right is invalidated] because the reason is removed by another reason, so he may nullify it and take it just as he may nullify all his other dispositions, even the mosque and cemetery. And the house is not sold for the debt of the buyer because the right of the pre-emptor takes precedence over the right of the buyer. If the judge or his executor sells it for a debt of the deceased, the pre-emptor may nullify such sale just as if the buyer had sold it during his lifetime. It cannot be said: 'The sale of the judge is his ruling, so how can it be nullified?' because it is a ruling contrary to consensus (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:278). (Fath) 'And by selling what is the basis for shuf'ah etc.' means: Pre-emption is invalidated by selling the house which is the basis for shuf'ah before the decision, i.e., before the judge's ruling on shuf'ah. There is no difference between whether he knew at the time of selling his house of the purchase of the property subject to shuf'ah or not; because it does not differ in the two states, so it becomes like an explicit surrender. Similarly, it does not differ whether he knew of its sale or not. Likewise, releasing the debtor from obligation (ibra' al-gharim) [invalidates] because all that is dropping (isqat), so it does not depend on knowledge just like divorce and freeing a slave (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:343). And if he sells the house which is the basis for shuf'ah with an option (khiyar), his shuf'ah is not invalidated. And if the pre-emptor buys it from the buyer, his shuf'ah is invalidated because by proceeding to buy he turned away. Likewise, if the pre-emptor rents it or haggles over it, it is invalidated because it is evidence of turning away. And if the pre-emptor makes the property which is the basis for shuf'ah into a mosque, cemetery, or endowment before he is awarded shuf'ah, his shuf'ah is invalidated (Multaqat). 'And there is no shuf'ah for one who sold etc.' means: There is no shuf'ah for the one who sold by agency, i.e., if the pre-emptor is the agent of the seller of the property subject to shuf'ah; because he is authorized to complete the sale, and in taking by shuf'ah he would be voiding what he was authorized to complete. And according to the three (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), shuf'ah is established for him. Likewise, there is no shuf'ah for the one for whom the property was sold (bīʿa lahu): the pronoun refers to 'man' (whoever). That is, if the seller is a mudarib and the pre-emptor is the owner of capital (rabb al-mal), and he sold the house, the owner of capital has no shuf'ah in it because the sale is for him. Similarly, if a slave who is authorized to trade sold a house, the master has no shuf'ah in it for what we have mentioned (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:343). 'Or he guaranteed the darak etc.' means: If the pre-emptor guarantees the seller against liability (al-darak), i.e., what the seller must pay after eviction due to a prior right, then he has no shuf'ah; because the completion of the sale is from his side, so he may not nullify what is completed from his side (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:379). Because by guaranteeing darak, he guarantees that the house will be obtained, which cannot be without him dropping the shuf'ah, and in taking it he would void that. According to the three, shuf'ah is established for him. ('Ayni) 'And whoever bought etc.' means: Whoever buys or for whom a purchase is made has shuf'ah. As for if he buys by way of agency and he is a pre-emptor, he has shuf'ah because it does not involve voiding his purchase, but rather it establishes it; because taking by shuf'ah is like taking by purchase. And if a purchase is made for him, similarly, like a mudarib who buys a property from the mudaraba capital, and the owner of capital is its pre-emptor, the owner of capital has shuf'ah. Likewise, if an authorized slave buys, the master has shuf'ah if the slave has debt; if there is no debt, there is no benefit in taking because he owns it, and no right of others attaches to it (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:344).
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage covers a set of interrelated rulings. First, selling the property that gives rise to shuf'ah (e.g., the shared wall or adjacent land) before the court awards the pre-emption invalidates the right, because it shows the pre-emptor has abandoned his claim. This applies even if he was unaware of the purchase at the time of sale, as it is treated as an explicit surrender. Similarly, releasing a debtor (ibra') also invalidates. However, selling with an option (khiyar) does not invalidate because the sale is not final. If the pre-emptor himself buys the property from the buyer, his shuf'ah is invalidated as it shows abandonment. Renting or haggling also invalidates. Additionally, converting the basis property into a mosque, cemetery, or endowment before the court decision invalidates shuf'ah. Second, the text discusses who loses shuf'ah: a person who sells the property as an agent for the seller; or for whom the property is sold (e.g., the investor in a mudaraba); or who guarantees the seller's liability (darak). In these cases, taking shuf'ah would contradict their own role in the sale. The three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) disagree and grant shuf'ah to such persons. Third, the text states who has shuf'ah: the person who buys directly or for whom the purchase is made (e.g., the investor when the mudarib buys). Also, if a pre-emptor buys as an agent, he still has shuf'ah because taking by shuf'ah does not void his purchase; it is similar to buying directly. For a slave who buys with permission, the master has shuf'ah only if the slave has debts; otherwise the master already owns the property through the slave's acquisition.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to take over a sale of an adjacent property by paying the price.
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option: a condition in a contract allowing a party to rescind the sale within a specified period.
الإعراض (al-i'rad): Turning away: an act indicating the pre-emptor has abandoned his right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following does NOT invalidate the right of shuf'ah?
A) Selling the basis property unconditionally
B) Renting the basis property
C) Selling the basis property with an option
D) Converting the basis property into a mosque

Key Takeaway
Selling, renting, or converting the property that gives shuf'ah before the court decision invalidates the right, regardless of the pre-emptor's knowledge, except when the sale is conditional (khiyar).
