Who Has No Shuf'ah: Agent and Beneficiary – Hanafi vs. Three Imams
Inter-madhab dispute on whether agent/beneficiary of a sale retains shuf'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which persons have no shuf'ah according to the Hanafi school, explain the Hanafi reasoning, and compare it with the position of the three Imams.

Core Idea
An agent who sold or a beneficiary of a sale has no shuf'ah, but a buyer's agent retains it.

The right of shuf'ah does not belong to everyone. The Hanafi school identifies specific individuals who, due to their involvement in the sale, are disqualified from claiming pre-emption. This lesson focuses on the agent who sold the property, the beneficiary for whom the property was sold (e.g., the capital owner in a mudaraba), and the guarantor of darak. The three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) disagree, granting shuf'ah to these individuals. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi principle that one cannot both complete a sale and then void it by exercising shuf'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify who has no shuf'ah
From the text, three categories: (1) the person who sold the property as an agent (وكيل البائع), (2) the person for whom the property was sold (e.g., the capital owner when the mudarib sells), (3) the person who guaranteed the seller's liability (ضامن الدرك).
2
Understand the Hanafi reasoning
The text argues that these persons are directly involved in completing the sale. Taking shuf'ah would mean nullifying what they themselves completed or authorized. For example, an agent who sold cannot then undo the sale.
3
Contrast with the three Imams
The three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that shuf'ah is an independent right unaffected by the person's role in the sale. They maintain that the agent or beneficiary still retains shuf'ah.
4
Apply to the buyer's side
Contrast this with the person who bought or for whom a purchase was made. The text clarifies that such persons do have shuf'ah because taking by shuf'ah does not void the purchase; it is similar to buying directly.
Example: a mudarib who buys from the mudaraba capital – the capital owner has shuf'ah.
5
Practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: in the Hanafi school, agents selling and beneficiaries of sales have no shuf'ah; in the other schools, they do. This affects litigation and fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبطل أيضاً وببيع ما يشفع به قبل القضاء بالشفعة، ولا شفعة لمن باع أو بيع له، أو ضمن عن البائع، ومن ابتاع أو ابتيع له، فله الشفعة، أي الاستحقاق الدرك لزوال سبب بسبب آخر، فينقضه ويأخذها كما ينقض سائر تصرفاته حتى المسجد ،والمقبرة ولا تباع الدار في دين المشتري؛ لأن حق الشفيع مقدم على حق المشتري، فإن باعها القاضي أو وصيه في دين الميت، فللشفيع أن ينقضه كما لو باعها المشتري في حياته لا يقال: بيع القاضي حكم منه، فكيف ينقض؛ لأنه قضاء منه مخالف للإجماع. تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۸/۸] (فتح) وببيع ما يشفع به إلخ يعني تبطل الشفعة ببيع الدار التي يشفع بها قبل القضاء أي قبل حكم الحاكم بالشفعة؛ الاستحقاق، ولا فرق بين أن يكون عالما وقت بيع داره بشراء المشفوعة، أو لم يكن عالما؛ لأنه لا يختلف في الحالين فصار كالتسليم الصريح، فإنه لا يختلف بين أن يعلم بيعها أو لم يعلم وكذا إبراء الغريم؛ لأن كل ذلك إسقاط، فلا يتوقف على العلم كالطلاق والعتاق. رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٣/٢] ولو باع التي يشفع بها بشرط الخيار لا تبطل شفعته ولو اشتراها الشفيع من المشتري بطلت شفعته؛ لأنه بالإقدام على الشراء أعرض عن وكذا إذا استأجرها الشفيع، أو ساومها بطلت؛ لأنه دليل الإعراض، ولو جعل الشفيع ما يشفع به مسجدا أو مقبرة أو وقفا قبل أن يقضي له بالشفعة، بطلت شفعته ملتقط . ولا شفعة لمن باع إلخ أي لا شفعة للذي باع بالوكالة بأن كان الشفيع وكيلا لبائع الدار المشفوعة؛ لأنه موكل بإتمام البيع، وفي أخذه بالشفعة نقض ما وكل بإتمامه وعند الثلاثة تجب له الشفعة، وكذا لا شفعة لمن والضمير في "له" يرجع إلى "من" يعنى بيع العقار لأجله بأن كان البائع مضاربا والشفيع رب المال فباع الدار، ليس لرب المال الشفعة فيها؛ لأن البيع له، وكذا إذا كان عبدا مأذونا له في التجارة فباع الدار ليس للمولى الشفعة فيها لما ذكرنا رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٣/٢] أو ضمن الدرك إلخ: أي إذا ضمن الشفيع عن البائع الدرك وهو ما يلزم البائع بعد الاستحقاق، فلا شفعة له؛ لأن تمام البيع إنما كان من جهته، فليس له أن ينقض ما تم من جهته تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۷۹/۸] لأنه بضمانه الدرك ضمن له أن تحصل له الدار، وذلك لا يكون إلا بتركه الشفعة وفي أخذه بها إبطال ذلك، وعند الثلاثة: تحب. (عيني) ومن ابتاع إلخ: أي من اشترى أو اشتري لأجله فله الشفعة، أما إذا اشترى بطريق الوكالة وهو شفيع، الشفعة؛ لأنه ليس فيه إبطال شرائه، بل فيه تقرير من جهته؛ لأن الأخذ بالشفعة مثل الأخذ بالشراء، وأما إذا اشتري له فكذلك كالمضارب إذا اشترى عقارا من مال المضاربة، ورب المال شفيعها، كان لرب المال الشفعة، وكذا العبد المأذون إذا اشترى فللمولى الشفعة إذا كان على العبد دين وإن لم يكن عليه دين فلا فائدة بالأخذ؛ لأنه ملكه، ولم يتعلق به حق الغير [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/٢]
It is also invalidated by selling what is the basis for shuf'ah before the court decision on shuf'ah, and there is no shuf'ah for one who sold or for whom a sale was made, or who guaranteed the seller against liability (al-darak), and whoever bought or for whom a purchase was made, he has shuf'ah, i.e., the right of acquisition. [The right is invalidated] because the reason is removed by another reason, so he may nullify it and take it just as he may nullify all his other dispositions, even the mosque and cemetery. And the house is not sold for the debt of the buyer because the right of the pre-emptor takes precedence over the right of the buyer. If the judge or his executor sells it for a debt of the deceased, the pre-emptor may nullify such sale just as if the buyer had sold it during his lifetime. It cannot be said: 'The sale of the judge is his ruling, so how can it be nullified?' because it is a ruling contrary to consensus (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:278). (Fath) 'And by selling what is the basis for shuf'ah etc.' means: Pre-emption is invalidated by selling the house which is the basis for shuf'ah before the decision, i.e., before the judge's ruling on shuf'ah. There is no difference between whether he knew at the time of selling his house of the purchase of the property subject to shuf'ah or not; because it does not differ in the two states, so it becomes like an explicit surrender. Similarly, it does not differ whether he knew of its sale or not. Likewise, releasing the debtor from obligation (ibra' al-gharim) [invalidates] because all that is dropping (isqat), so it does not depend on knowledge just like divorce and freeing a slave (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:343). And if he sells the house which is the basis for shuf'ah with an option (khiyar), his shuf'ah is not invalidated. And if the pre-emptor buys it from the buyer, his shuf'ah is invalidated because by proceeding to buy he turned away. Likewise, if the pre-emptor rents it or haggles over it, it is invalidated because it is evidence of turning away. And if the pre-emptor makes the property which is the basis for shuf'ah into a mosque, cemetery, or endowment before he is awarded shuf'ah, his shuf'ah is invalidated (Multaqat). 'And there is no shuf'ah for one who sold etc.' means: There is no shuf'ah for the one who sold by agency, i.e., if the pre-emptor is the agent of the seller of the property subject to shuf'ah; because he is authorized to complete the sale, and in taking by shuf'ah he would be voiding what he was authorized to complete. And according to the three (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), shuf'ah is established for him. Likewise, there is no shuf'ah for the one for whom the property was sold (bīʿa lahu): the pronoun refers to 'man' (whoever). That is, if the seller is a mudarib and the pre-emptor is the owner of capital (rabb al-mal), and he sold the house, the owner of capital has no shuf'ah in it because the sale is for him. Similarly, if a slave who is authorized to trade sold a house, the master has no shuf'ah in it for what we have mentioned (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:343). 'Or he guaranteed the darak etc.' means: If the pre-emptor guarantees the seller against liability (al-darak), i.e., what the seller must pay after eviction due to a prior right, then he has no shuf'ah; because the completion of the sale is from his side, so he may not nullify what is completed from his side (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:379). Because by guaranteeing darak, he guarantees that the house will be obtained, which cannot be without him dropping the shuf'ah, and in taking it he would void that. According to the three, shuf'ah is established for him. ('Ayni) 'And whoever bought etc.' means: Whoever buys or for whom a purchase is made has shuf'ah. As for if he buys by way of agency and he is a pre-emptor, he has shuf'ah because it does not involve voiding his purchase, but rather it establishes it; because taking by shuf'ah is like taking by purchase. And if a purchase is made for him, similarly, like a mudarib who buys a property from the mudaraba capital, and the owner of capital is its pre-emptor, the owner of capital has shuf'ah. Likewise, if an authorized slave buys, the master has shuf'ah if the slave has debt; if there is no debt, there is no benefit in taking because he owns it, and no right of others attaches to it (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:344).
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage covers a set of interrelated rulings. First, selling the property that gives rise to shuf'ah (e.g., the shared wall or adjacent land) before the court awards the pre-emption invalidates the right, because it shows the pre-emptor has abandoned his claim. This applies even if he was unaware of the purchase at the time of sale, as it is treated as an explicit surrender. Similarly, releasing a debtor (ibra') also invalidates. However, selling with an option (khiyar) does not invalidate because the sale is not final. If the pre-emptor himself buys the property from the buyer, his shuf'ah is invalidated as it shows abandonment. Renting or haggling also invalidates. Additionally, converting the basis property into a mosque, cemetery, or endowment before the court decision invalidates shuf'ah. Second, the text discusses who loses shuf'ah: a person who sells the property as an agent for the seller; or for whom the property is sold (e.g., the investor in a mudaraba); or who guarantees the seller's liability (darak). In these cases, taking shuf'ah would contradict their own role in the sale. The three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) disagree and grant shuf'ah to such persons. Third, the text states who has shuf'ah: the person who buys directly or for whom the purchase is made (e.g., the investor when the mudarib buys). Also, if a pre-emptor buys as an agent, he still has shuf'ah because taking by shuf'ah does not void his purchase; it is similar to buying directly. For a slave who buys with permission, the master has shuf'ah only if the slave has debts; otherwise the master already owns the property through the slave's acquisition.

Key Terms
الدرك (al-darak): Liability of the seller to indemnify the buyer if the property is later claimed by a third party with a superior right.
المضاربة (al-mudarabah): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other manages the business.
الوكالة (al-wikala): Agency: representing another in a legal transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following persons has the right of shuf'ah?
A) The agent who sold the property for the seller
B) The owner of capital (rabb al-mal) when the mudarib sells a house from the mudaraba
C) The person who guaranteed the seller's liability (darak)
D) The owner of capital when the mudarib buys a house from the mudaraba

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a person who sold as agent, for whom a sale was made, or who guaranteed the seller's liability has no shuf'ah, but a person who bought as agent or for whom a purchase was made retains it.

Selling Except a Cubit: When Shuf'ah is Lost
Ruling with talil: why retaining a cubit adjacent to the neighbor eliminates shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a property while retaining a small strip adjacent to the shafi', identify the illa (reason) based on contiguity, and explain the hila (legal stratagem) used to eliminate shuf'ah.

Core Idea
Retaining a single cubit adjacent to the neighbor severs contiguity and drops the right of pre-emption.

Shuf'ah (pre-emption) is a right of a neighbor or co-owner to step into a sale. But what if the seller wants to sell to a third party without granting that right? The Hanafi school recognizes a legal stratagem: the seller retains a small strip of land adjacent to the neighbor. This lesson examines the ruling, the reasoning based on contiguity, and the ethical dimension of such stratagems.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its effect on shuf'ah
The act is selling the whole property except for a small strip (one cubit) that is adjacent to the shafi' (the person who would have the right of pre-emption). The effect is that the shuf'ah right is completely nullified.
Shuf'ah does not arise at all; it is not a matter of the shafi' losing an already established right.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is a rational argument based on the definition of shuf'ah. The text states: 'because entitlement is by adjacency, and there is no contiguity with the sold property.' This is a talil (reasoning) not a specific textual proof.
The 'illa is the absence of contiguity (ittisal) between the shafi''s property and the sold property.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key phrase is 'because entitlement is by adjacency' (li-anna al-istiḥqāqa bi-al-jiwār). This shows that contiguity is a condition for shuf'ah. When the seller retains the adjacent strip, the sold property no longer touches the shafi''s property, so the condition fails.
The illa is mulasama (contiguity). The ruling applies universally: any retained portion adjacent to the shafi' severs contiguity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools, especially Shafi'i, may not accept this hila because they limit shuf'ah to co-owners or reject hiyal that defeat a right that would otherwise vest. The Hanafi school, however, permits it as a valid exercise of ownership before the right vests.
The difference is rooted in the definition of shuf'ah and the permissibility of hiyal.
5
Evaluate the hila
The text explicitly says 'this is a legal stratagem to drop shuf'ah.' In Hanafi fiqh, hiyal are permissible if they do not involve deception or violate a definitive ruling. Here, the seller is simply exercising his right to retain a portion of his property, which prevents shuf'ah from arising.
The hila is valid only if performed before the shuf'ah right becomes established (i.e., before the sale is complete).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن باعها إلا ذراعا في جانب الشفيع فلا شفعة له؛ لأن الاستحقاق بالجوار، ولم يوجد الاتصال بالمبيع، وكذا لو وهب هذا القدر للمشتري؛ لعدم الاتصال، وهذه حيلة لإسقاط الشفعة.
And if he sold it except a cubit on the side of the shafi', then there is no shuf'ah for him, because entitlement is by adjacency, and there is no contiguity with the sold property; and similarly if he gifted this amount to the buyer due to lack of contiguity; and this is a legal stratagem to drop shuf'ah.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This is a ruling statement on what invalidates the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The author explains that if the seller sells the entire property except for a small strip (one cubit) that is adjacent to the neighbor who would have the right of shuf'ah, then that neighbor loses his right of pre-emption. The reason ('illa) is that the right of shuf'ah is based on the neighbor's property being contiguous (mulasima) with the sold property. Since the seller retained a strip adjacent to the neighbor, the sold property is not contiguous with the neighbor's property. The same ruling applies if the seller gives that strip as a gift to the buyer after the sale. This is considered a legal stratagem (hila) to eliminate the shuf'ah right. The Hanafi school permits such stratagems when done before the right of shuf'ah becomes established, as will be discussed later.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of a neighbor or co-owner to purchase a property that has been sold to a third party by pre-empting the buyer, for the same price and conditions.
الجوار (al-jiwar): Adjacency or neighborliness; the state of properties being contiguous along the boundary line.
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem that uses the apparent structure of a contract to achieve a different result, not prohibited if it does not involve deception or violation of a definitive ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the seller sells the entire property except for one cubit adjacent to the neighbor who would have shuf'ah, what happens to the shuf'ah right?
A) The neighbor still has shuf'ah for the entire sold property.
B) The neighbor has shuf'ah only for the part not adjacent to the retained strip.
C) The neighbor's shuf'ah is completely nullified because there is no contiguity.
D) The neighbor must buy the retained strip along with the sold property.

Key Takeaway
Retaining a strip adjacent to the shafi' severs contiguity and nullifies the right of pre-emption in the Hanafi school.

Shuf'ah for a Neighbor When a Buyer Purchases in Stages
Ruling with reasoning: limiting neighbor's shuf'ah due to buyer's partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a partner's pre-emption right supersedes a neighbor's in staged purchases.

Core Idea
A partner always outranks a neighbor in shuf'ah.

This lesson examines a common scenario in pre-emption law: a buyer acquires a share of a property, then later buys the remaining shares. The neighbor who initially had a right of shuf'ah finds that right limited. The key to understanding the ruling is the buyer's transformation into a partner after the first purchase. In Hanafi law, a partner's right to pre-empt is stronger than a neighbor's, so the neighbor can only enforce shuf'ah on the first share.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two transactions
Note that the buyer first purchases one share (sahm) from the property, and later purchases the remainder (baqiyatiha). These are two separate transactions.
The order matters: first purchase, then second.
2
Determine the neighbor's shuf'ah right on the first share
At the time of the first purchase, the buyer is not yet a partner (only a buyer). The neighbor's right of shuf'ah because of adjacency applies to this share.
The neighbor can potentially take this share by paying the price.
3
Recognize the buyer's partnership after the first purchase
After completing the first purchase, the buyer becomes a co-owner (sharik) in the property for that share.
This status is crucial for the second transaction.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule of priority for shuf'ah
When the buyer later buys the remaining shares, he is now a partner in the property. In Hanafi law, a partner has stronger rights to pre-empt than a neighbor. Therefore, the partner's right supersedes the neighbor's.
The buyer, as a partner, pre-empts the remaining shares himself.
5
Conclude the neighbor's limited right
The neighbor's shuf'ah is only valid for the first share. He cannot take the later shares because the buyer's partnership gives him priority.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ابتاع منها سهما بثمن، ثم ابتاع بقيتها، فالشفعة للجار في السهم الأول فقط، ولا شفعة له في الباقي؛ لأن المشتري في الثاني شريك، فيقدم على الجار.
And if he bought from it a share for a price, then bought its remainder, then the shuf'ah is for the neighbor in the first share only, and there is no shuf'ah for him in the remainder, because the buyer in the second [purchase] is a partner, so he is given precedence over the neighbor.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This ruling addresses a scenario where a buyer purchases one share of a property (e.g., from a co-owner) and later purchases the remaining shares. The neighbor who had a right of shuf'ah (because of adjacency) can only take the first share via shuf'ah. He cannot take the subsequent shares because after the first purchase, the buyer becomes a partner (sharik) in the property. In Hanafi law, a partner has a stronger right to pre-empt than a mere neighbor. Thus, when the buyer buys the remaining shares, he is pre-empting as a partner, and the neighbor's right is superseded. The commentary clarifies different scenarios: if the neighbor learns of both sales simultaneously, or learns of the second before taking the first, the buyer still prevails because his partnership status is established before the neighbor's shuf'ah is exercised.

Key Terms
السهم (al-sahm): A share or portion of a property that is undivided; a part of a jointly owned property.
الشريك (al-sharik): A co-owner or partner who owns a part of the same property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer buys one share of a property, then later buys the remaining shares, what is the neighbor's shuf'ah right?
A) The neighbor can take all shares via shuf'ah.
B) The neighbor can only take the first share via shuf'ah because the buyer becomes a partner for the rest.
C) The neighbor cannot take any share because the buyer is a partner from the start.
D) The neighbor's shuf'ah is delayed until the buyer completes the purchase.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a partner's right of pre-emption supersedes a neighbor's right, so a neighbor's shuf'ah applies only to the first share when a buyer purchases in stages.

Shuf'ah with Payment in Kind: The Price, Not the Substitute
Ruling on shuf'ah with substitute payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on shuf'ah when the buyer pays with a substitute, and explain why the pre-emptor pays the price, not the substitute.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is based on the original price; the substitute payment is a separate transaction.

When a buyer purchases property but gives the seller a garment (or any item) instead of cash, the right of shuf'ah (pre-emption) remains attached to the original price. The pre-emptor steps into the buyer's shoes and must pay that price—not the garment. This lesson explains the reasoning and practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The buyer agrees to a sale for a certain price but hands over a garment (or other item) as payment instead of money. Recognize that this is a payment in kind, not a simple exchange.
2
State the Ruling
The rule: al-shuf'ah bil-thaman la bil-thawb—the pre-emptor pays the price, not the garment. This is the direct teaching from the text.
3
Analyze the Reasoning
The garment is a substitute (iwad) for what the buyer owes in his liability (dhimma). By accepting it, the seller effectively buys the garment in a separate contract. The original sale contract remains unchanged, so the pre-emptor's right relates to that original price.
This reasoning is a talil (causal explanation) based on the independence of the transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دفع ثوبا عنه، فالشفعة بالثمن لا الثوب؛ لأن الثوب عوض عما في ذمة المشتري، فيكون البائع مشتريا للثوب بعقد آخر غير العقد الأول.
Then, if he gives a garment in exchange for it, then the shuf'ah is with the price, not the garment, because the garment is a substitute for what is in the buyer's liability; so the seller becomes a buyer of the garment by a separate contract, not the first contract.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This ruling deals with a scenario where a buyer purchases property but instead of paying the agreed price in money, he gives the seller a garment (or any other item) in lieu of the price. The right of shuf'ah is calculated based on the original price (thaman), not the garment. The reason is that the garment is not part of the original sale; it is a separate transaction (a sale of the garment to the seller) used to settle the debt. The shafi' (pre-emptor) steps into the shoes of the buyer in the original sale, so he pays the original price. The commentary notes that this is a hila to discourage the neighbor from pre-empting (because he would have to pay the full price, while the buyer effectively paid less via the garment). However, this hila has risks: if the property is later found to be usurped, the buyer may be held for the full price. The text suggests a better hila: pay with gold coins (dinar) instead of silver (dirham) to create a different contract.

Key Terms
الثمن (al-thaman): The price agreed upon in the original sale contract.
العوض (al-'iwad): A substitute or compensation; here, the garment given in place of the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer agrees to buy a house for 1000 dirhams, but instead of paying the dirhams he gives the seller a garment worth 1000 dirhams, how much must the shafi' pay to exercise shuf'ah?
A) The garment itself.
B) 1000 dirhams (the price).
C) Nothing, because the transaction is invalid.
D) Either the garment or the dirhams at his choice.

Key Takeaway
In shuf'ah, the pre-emptor pays the original price, not any substitute given by the buyer.

The Permissibility of Legal Stratagems in Shuf'ah: A Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on permissibility of hiyal to drop shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the use of hiyal to drop shuf'ah and state the mufta bihi position for shuf'ah.

Core Idea
Using a legal stratagem to avoid shuf'ah is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf, but disliked according to Muhammad — the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a nuanced internal Hanafi debate: is it permissible to use a legal stratagem (hila) to preemptively drop the right of shuf'ah before it becomes established? The two great disciples of Abu Hanifa — Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani — differ on the ruling, each grounding their position in the concept of harm (darar). By studying this dispute, you will learn how the Hanafi school resolves conflicting principles when the same underlying value (preventing harm) points in opposite directions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions and their legal classification
Note that Abu Yusuf says it is not makruh (i.e., permissible), while Muhammad says it is makruh (disliked). Recognize that the disputed ruling is about using a hila before shuf'ah becomes wajib.
After shuf'ah becomes wajib, hiyal are unanimously impermissible.
2
Analyze Muhammad's reasoning
Muhammad argues that shuf'ah was legislated to repel harm from the neighbor. Repelling harm is wajib, so causing harm to the neighbor is haram. Therefore, a hila that results in harm to the neighbor is necessarily makruh.
Muhammad's view emphasizes the obligatory nature of shuf'ah as a right of the neighbor.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf argues that the seller is using the hila to repel harm from himself (the forced loss or inconvenience). Repelling harm from oneself is lawful, even if it causes harm to another. The seller's self-defense outweighs the neighbor's potential harm.
Abu Yusuf balances two harms: the seller's harm vs. neighbor's harm, prioritizing the seller's right to protect his interest.
4
Apply the tarjih and note the distinction between shuf'ah and zakat
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-'Ayni) gave fatwa according to Abu Yusuf for shuf'ah but according to Muhammad for zakat. Understand that the context of the right matters: shuf'ah is a property right based on partnership/neighborhood, while zakat is a worship-based obligation, making hiyal more suspect in worship.
This distinction shows that tarjih can vary by subject even within the same type of hila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تكره الحيلة لإسقاط الشفعة والزكاة، وأخذ حظ البعض بتعدد المشتري، لا بتعدد البائع. هذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد: نكره؛ لأن الشفعة وجبت لدفع الضرر، وهو واجب، وإلحاق الضرر به حرام، فكانت مكروهة ضرورة، ولأبي يوسف: أنه يحتاج لدفع الضرر عن نفسه، والحيلة لدفع الضرر عن نفسه مشروع، وإن كان غيره يتضرر بذلك.
And the legal stratagem to drop shuf'ah and zakat is not disliked, [as for] taking the share of some when there are multiple buyers, not when there are multiple sellers. This is according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad: we dislike it because shuf'ah was established to repel harm, and repelling harm is obligatory, and causing harm to him is prohibited, so it is disliked by necessity. For Abu Yusuf: he needs to repel harm from himself, and a stratagem to repel harm from oneself is lawful, even if others are harmed by it.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the permissibility of using legal stratagems (hiyal) to drop the right of shuf'ah (and zakat). Abu Yusuf holds that such hiyal are not disliked because the person is protecting himself from harm (the loss of property or having to buy an unwanted share). Muhammad holds that they are disliked because shuf'ah is prescribed to prevent harm to the neighbor, and using a hila to deprive the neighbor of his right is disliked. The commentator notes that later Hanafis issued fatwa in shuf'ah according to Abu Yusuf (permitting hiyal) and in zakat according to Muhammad (disliking). The passage also distinguishes between two types of hiyal: one after shuf'ah becomes wajib (impermissible by agreement) and one before (the subject of dispute).

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem that uses permissible contracts to achieve a different result, here to avoid the right of shuf'ah.
الضرر (al-darar): Harm or injury; the basis for the legislation of shuf'ah is to prevent harm to the neighbor/partner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding hiyal to drop shuf'ah?
A) Abu Yusuf allows them; Muhammad prohibits them completely.
B) Both allow them but with different conditions.
C) Abu Yusuf does not consider them makruh; Muhammad considers them makruh (disliked).
D) Both consider them makruh, but Muhammad less so.

Key Takeaway
For shuf'ah, the Hanafi fatwa permits using legal stratagems before the right becomes established, following Abu Yusuf, though Muhammad considers it disliked.

Taking Share of Some Buyers vs. Sellers in Shuf'ah
Ruling: shuf'ah can be taken from some buyers but not from some sellers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on taking partial shares in shuf'ah when there are multiple buyers or multiple sellers, and explain the underlying rationale (darar).

Core Idea
Shuf'ah cannot be exercised partially against a single buyer when sellers are multiple, to prevent extra harm to the buyer.

This passage clarifies a key distinction in shuf'ah (pre-emption) law. When a property is bought by multiple buyers, the pre-emptor (shafi') may take the share of some buyers and leave others. But when a jointly-owned property is sold by multiple sellers to a single buyer, the shafi' must take the entire property or none. The rationale is that shuf'ah, an exception to normal property rights, should not cause additional harm (darar) to the buyer by forcing him into an unwanted partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two situations: (1) multiple buyers from one seller, and (2) multiple sellers to one buyer. Recognize that the number of parties on the buying side matters.
2
State the ruling for each
In scenario (1), the shafi' may take the share of some buyers and leave others. In scenario (2), the shafi' cannot take a partial share; he must take the entire property or none.
3
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The text gives the reason: taking part in scenario (2) would split the contract (tafarruq al-safqah) and cause extra harm to the buyer by making him a partner with the shafi'. In scenario (1), the shafi' steps into the shoes of one buyer, so no splitting occurs.
4
Apply the principle of harm
Shuf'ah was legislated as an exception to remove harm from the shafi'. Therefore, it should not be extended in a way that causes greater harm to the buyer. This is the basis for the distinction.
5
Note the practical consequence
The ruling means that a shafi' must assess whether the transaction involves multiple buyers or multiple sellers before deciding to claim only a part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخذ حظ البعض بتعدد المشتري، لا بتعدد البائع. صورته: أن المشتري إذا تعدد بأن اشترى جماعة عقارا، والبائع واحد، يتعدد الأخذ بالشفعة بتعددهم حتى كان للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب بعضهم، ويترك الباقي، وإن تعدد البائع بأن باع جماعة عقارا مشتركا بينهم والمشتري واحد لا يتعدد الأخذ بالشفعة بتعددهم، حتى لا يكون للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب بعضهم دون بعض. والفرق أن في الوجه الثاني بأخذ البعض تتفرق الصفقة على المشتري، فيتضرر بعيب الشركة والشفعة شرعت على خلاف القياس لدفع الضرر عن الشفيع فلا تشرع على وجه يتضرر به المشتري ضررا زائدا على الأخذ بالشفعة، وفي الوجه الأول يقوم الشفيع مقام أحدهم، فلا تتفرق الصفقة.
And taking the share of some [from] multiple buyers, not from multiple sellers. Its example: when the buyers are multiple, such that a group purchases a property and there is one seller, the taking by shuf'ah is multiplied by their multiplicity, such that the shafi' can take the share of some of them and leave the rest. And if the sellers are multiple, such that a group sells a jointly owned property and there is one buyer, the taking by shuf'ah is not multiplied by their multiplicity, such that the shafi' cannot take the share of some of them without others. The difference is that in the second case, taking some splits the contract (safqah) upon the buyer, causing him extra harm due to defective partnership, and shuf'ah was legislated contrary to analogy to repel harm from the shafi', so it should not be legislated in a way that causes extra harm to the buyer beyond the taking of shuf'ah. In the first case, the shafi' stands in the place of one of them, so the contract is not split.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) multiple buyers purchasing from one seller, and (2) multiple sellers selling to one buyer. In the first scenario, the shafi' (neighbor or partner) may choose to take the share of some of the buyers and leave others. This is because the shafi' steps into the shoes of one buyer, each buyer having a separate contract (though in reality it might be one contract, the Hanafi view considers it as multiple for purposes of shuf'ah). In the second scenario, if there are multiple sellers of a jointly owned property sold to one buyer, the shafi' cannot take only a part; he must take the whole or none. The reason is that if he took only a part, the remaining part would cause the buyer to become a partner with the shafi', which would harm the buyer (since partnership in property is a defect). Since shuf'ah was already an exception to general principles (qiyas) to remove harm from the shafi', it should not be extended to cause extra harm to the buyer. This ruling applies whether the sale is before or after delivery (qabd) according to the correct position.

Key Terms
الصفقة (al-safqah): The contract or transaction; here, the unity of the transaction is considered.
الشفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by compensating the buyer.
الضرر (al-darar): Harm; the key principle in this ruling is that shuf'ah should not cause extra harm beyond its purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two brothers jointly sell a house to a single buyer, and the neighbor wants to exercise shuf'ah, can the neighbor take only one brother's share?
A) Yes, he can take one brother's share and leave the other.
B) No, he must take the entire house or none.
C) He can take whichever share he wants, but must pay extra.
D) He cannot take any share because there are multiple sellers.

Key Takeaway
In shuf'ah, the shafi' may take a partial share only when buyers are multiple; when sellers are multiple, he must take all or none, to avoid extra harm to the buyer.

Pre-emption Rights of an Indebted Authorized Slave
Ruling on a ma'dhun slave with debt taking pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on shuf'a for a ma'dhun slave with debt and explain the legal reasoning based on milk al-yad.

Core Idea
An indebted slave authorized to trade can take pre-emption because he acquires beneficial ownership.

In this passage, the author discusses a nuanced scenario: a slave who is permitted to trade but owes debts that exceed his assets. Normally, a slave's acquisitions belong to the master, but overwhelming debt shifts the beneficial ownership to the slave. Consequently, the slave may exercise the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) even against his master. We will unpack the scenario and the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
The seller of a house, his slave (ma'dhun), and debt covering the slave's property and person.
The master is the seller; the slave is a third party with a right of pre-emption.
2
State the ruling
The indebted slave can take shuf'a from his master (the seller) – meaning the slave can pre-empt the sale and take the house from the buyer.
This is the main rule derived from the proof.
3
Analyze the legal basis
Shuf'a is akin to purchase (shira'). The slave's purchase from his master is valid when the slave has debt because it gives milk al-yad (possessory ownership). The master does not own what the slave acquires due to the debt.
The proof is talil: the ruling is justified by the nature of shuf'a and the effect of debt.
4
Apply the opposite scenario
If the slave is the seller and has debt, then the master takes shuf'a. Contrast this with the case where the slave has no debt – then the master takes shuf'a in both buying and selling.
The presence of debt flips the entitlement between slave and master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعبد المأذون المديون الأخذ بالشفعة من سيّده كعكسه. صورته: رجل باع دارا، وللبائع عبد مأذون له في التجارة، وعليه دين يحيط بماله ورقبته فللعبد أن يأخذ الدار بالشفعة؛ لأن الأخذ بالشفعة بمنزلة الشراء، وشراء أحدهما من الآخر يجوز إذا كان على العبد دين؛ لأنه يفيد ملك اليد؛ لكون المولى لا يملك ما في يد العبد المديون، أو لكون العبد أحق به. كعكسه: أي كعكس الحكم المذكور، وهو ما إذا كان العبد هو البائع وعليه دين فلمولاه أخذها بالشفعة كما ذكرنا، بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن عليه دين يحيط بماله ورقبته والعبد بائع؛ لأن بيعه لمولاه، ولا شفعة لمن بيع له على بخلاف ما إذا اشترى العبد فإنه لمولاه الشفعة؛ لأنه ابتيع له، وقد أن من ابتاع أو ابتيع له لا تبطل شفعته.
And the authorized slave who is indebted has the right to take pre-emption from his master, and its opposite. Its scenario: A man sold a house, and the seller had an authorized slave for trade who had debt encompassing his property and his person, so the slave may take the house by pre-emption, because taking by pre-emption is akin to purchase, and the purchase of one from the other is permissible when the slave has debt, because it gives ownership of possession, since the master does not own what is in the hand of the indebted slave, or because the slave is more entitled to it. "And its opposite": i.e., like the reverse of the mentioned ruling, which is when the slave is the seller and has debt, then his master takes it by pre-emption as we mentioned, contrary to when there is no debt encompassing his property and person and the slave is the seller, because his sale is for his master, and there is no pre-emption for the one for whom it is sold, unlike when the slave buys, then his master has pre-emption because it was bought for him, and it has been narrated that one who buys or for whom a purchase is made, his pre-emption is not nullified.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation regarding pre-emption rights involving a slave (abd) who is authorized to trade (ma'dhun) and is burdened with debt that covers his entire property and even his person (i.e., his value). The key point: such a slave can take pre-emption from his master in a sale of a house, because taking shuf'a is like purchase, and the slave's purchase is valid though the master technically owns him, but because the slave's debt exceeds his assets, the master cannot claim ownership of what the slave acquires; rather, the slave acquires beneficial ownership (milk al-yad) for himself. Conversely, if the slave is the seller and has such debt, the master takes pre-emption, not the slave. If the slave does not have overwhelming debt, then the master takes pre-emption in both cases because the slave's transactions ultimately benefit the master. The passage clarifies that pre-emption rights are transferred accordingly.

Key Terms
العبد المأذون (al-'abd al-ma'dhun): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade.
ملك اليد (milk al-yad): Possessory ownership, as opposed to full ownership; the slave has beneficial possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, regardless of debt.
B) Only when the master sells property to someone else.
C) When the slave's debt covers his property and person, making him entitled to beneficial ownership.
D) Never, because the slave is not a legal owner.

Key Takeaway
An indebted ma'dhun slave can take pre-emption because his debt grants him beneficial ownership (milk al-yad), making him entitled to rights like purchase and shuf'a.

Definition and Legal Nature of Division (Qismah) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining division and its legal elements: cause, condition, pillar, ruling, and evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of qismah, identify its constituent elements (ifraz and mubadala), and distinguish the legal consequences for fungible versus non-fungible property.

Core Idea
Division is not merely segregation; it also involves an exchange element except in fungibles.

This lesson opens the Book of Division (Kitab al-Qismah). We will dissect the definition provided by the author, understand the legal components (cause, condition, pillar, ruling, evidence), and examine the distinctive Hanafi view that division combines segregation (ifraz) with exchange (mubadala). The practical difference matters: exchange triggers sale conditions when dividing non-fungible goods.

Guided Path
1
Parse the Linguistic and Legal Definition
Break down the phrase 'نصيب شائع في معين' – a joint share in a specified property. Note that before division, each partner owns a share in every part; division removes that commonality.
The definition applies to division of physical objects (a'yan), not benefits.
2
Identify the Two Components: Ifraz and Mubadala
Every part contains both partners' shares. When one receives a part, he takes his own right (ifraz) and also the partner's share in that part in exchange for what he left (mubadala). Understand why this duality exists.
3
List the Legal Elements: Sabab, Shart, Rukn, Hukm
Sabab = request of any partner. Shart = no loss of benefit after division. Rukn = any action distinguishing shares (measuring, weighing, etc.). Hukm = determination of each share separately.
If no request, division is invalid.
4
Examine the Evidences (Dalil)
Quran verses: 54:28, 4:8, 8:41. Sunnah: Prophet's practice in spoils and inheritances. Ijma. Relate each verse to the concept of division.
The evidence establishes the legitimacy of division in Islamic law.
5
Analyze the Madhhab Dispute on the Nature of Division
Hanafis (and Maliki for non-identical types) say division involves exchange; Shafi'is and Hanbalis say pure segregation. Trace the thamarah al-khilaf: sale conditions (riba etc.) apply only when exchange is present.
The Hanafi position: for fungibles (mithli), segregation dominates; for non-fungibles (qimi), exchange dominates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة

كتاب القسمة: أي هذا كتاب في بيان أحكام القسمة ووجه المناسبة بينه وبين كتاب الشفعة: أن الشفعة شرعت لدفع ضرر الجار، وتكميل منفعة الملك جبرا، فكذا القسمة شرعت لتكميل منفعة الملك، ويجري فيها الجبر إلا أن في الشفعة كمل معنى المبادلة فتقدم، وذكر الاتقاني أن وجه المناسبة من حيث إن كلا منهما من نتائج النصيب الشائع؛ لأن أقوى أسباب الشفعة الشركة في نفس المبيع، وتقدم الشفعة على القسمة؛ لأن التملك بالشفعة ربما يكون سببا للقسمة، والسبب مقدم على المسبب. (من) ملا مسكين وفتح الله المعين باختصار)
القسمة:
هي في اللغة: اسم للاقتسام كالقدوة للاقتداء ومعناها: رفع الشيوع وقطع الشركة، وفي الشرع ما بينه بقوله هي إلخ. (عيني) هي جمع نصيب إلخ: هذا معناه شرعا؛ لأنه ما من جزء معين إلا وهو مشتمل على النصيبين، فكان ما يقبضه كل واحد منهما نصفه ملكه ونصفه ملك صاحبه، فإذا وقعت القسمة صار حصة صاحبه فيما وقع في نصيبه عوضا عما فاته في نصيب صاحبه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٩٠/٨] واعلم أن القسمة نوعان: قسمة في الأعيان وقسمة في المنافع، وهذا التعريف الذي ذكره للقسمة في الأعيان، وأما القسمة في المنافع فلم يعرفها المصنف، وهي شرعا مبادلة المنفعة بجنسها، ثم لابد هنا من بيان ركن القسمة وسببها وشرطها وحكمها ودليل مشروعيتها، فركنها فعل يحصل به التمييز بين الأنصباء كالكيل والوزن والعد والزرع. وسببها: طلب الشركاء أو أحدهم الانتفاع بحصته حتى إذا لم يوجد الطلب، لا تصح القسمة. وشرطها: عدم فوت المنفعة بالقسمة، فإنها إفراز ما لكل واحد قبل القسمة الملك من والمنفعة، وإنما يتحقق هذا إذا بقي المفرز على ما كان قبل الإفراز بأصله ومنافعه وحكمها تعين نصيب كل واحد على حدة؛ لأنه الأثر المترتب عليها. ودليلها: الكتاب: وَتَبْتْهُمْ أَنَّ الْمَاءَ قِسْمَةٌ بَيْنَهُمْ ﴾ (القمر: ۲۸) ﴿وَإِذَا حَضَرَ الْقِسْمَةَ أُولُو الْقُرْبَى (النساء: ۸)، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا غَنِمْتُمْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ فَأَنَّ لِلَّهِ خُمُسَهُ وَلِلرَّسُولِ ﴾ (الأنفال: (٤١)، وإنما يعلم الخمس من أربعة الأخماس بالقسمة، والسنة: لمباشرته عليها لها في الغنائم والمواريث، والإجماع. (تكملة، فتح) على الإفراز والمبادلة: يعني أن القسمة تشتمل على معنى الإفراز وهو التمييز، وعلى معنى المبادلة؛ لأنه ما من جزء معين إلا وهو مشتمل على النصيبين، فكان ما يجتمع في نصيب أحدهما بعضه كان له وبعضه كان لصاحبه، فصار له عوضا عما في يد صاحبه فكان مبادلة من حيث أنه أخذ حق صاحبه في مقابلة حقه وإفرازا من حيث أنه يقبض حقه أيضا، هذا عندنا وعند مالك فيما اتحد جنسا وصفة ،إفراز، وفي غيره ،مبادلة، وعند الشافعي وأحمد: إفراز في الكل، وعن الشافعي: بيع في الكل. (مسكين، عيني) وهو الظاهر أي الإفراز هو الظاهر في المثلي كالمكيل والموزون والعددي المتقارب؛ لعدم التفاوت بين أبعاضه؛ لأن ما يأخذه كل واحد منهما من نصيب شريكه مثل حقه صورة ومعنى، فأمكن أن يجعل عين حقه، ولهذا جعل عين حقه في القرض والصرف والسلم. (كذا في الزيلعي والفتح)
Book of Division. Definition of Division and Its Rulings. Book of Division. Division: It is a joint share in a specified [property], and it comprises segregation and exchange, and this is the apparent [meaning]. 'Joint share': 'Division' and it is the distinction: 'Segregation'. Book of Division: i.e., this is a book explaining the rulings of division and the aspect of connection between it and the Book of Preemption: that preemption was legislated to repel the harm of the neighbor and to complete the benefit of ownership by compulsion; likewise, division was legislated to complete the benefit of ownership, and compulsion is applied in it, except that in preemption the meaning of exchange is complete, so it was mentioned first. Al-Itqani mentioned that the aspect of connection is that both are from the results of the joint share; because the strongest cause of preemption is partnership in the very thing sold, and preemption precedes division because ownership by preemption may be a cause for division, and the cause precedes the effect. (From Mulla Miskeen and Fath Allah al-Mu'in, abridged). Division: In language it is the name for sharing, like al-qudwa for following; its meaning is removal of commonality and cutting of partnership. In sacred law it is what he clarified by his saying 'it is' etc. (from al-'Ayni). 'It is the aggregate of shares' etc.: This is its legal meaning because there is no specified part that does not contain two shares; so what each one takes, half of it is his property and half is his partner's. When division occurs, his partner's share in what falls into his portion becomes compensation for what he missed in his partner's portion. (Completion from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/290). And know that division is of two types: division in physical objects and division in benefits. This definition mentioned by the author is for division in physical objects. As for division in benefits, he did not define it; it is legally exchange of a benefit for its like. Then here it is necessary to explain the pillar (rukn) of division, its cause, condition, ruling, and evidence of its legitimacy. Its pillar is an action by which distinction between shares is achieved, such as measuring, weighing, counting, and planting. Its cause is the request of the partners or one of them to benefit from his share, so that if no request exists, the division is not valid. Its condition is that benefit is not lost by division; because it is segregation of what each one already owns in terms of property and benefit, and this is realized only if the segregated part remains as it was before segregation in its essence and benefits. Its ruling is the determination of each one's share separately, because it is the effect resulting from it. Its evidence from the Book: His saying: 'And inform them that the water is a division between them' (al-Qamar 28); and 'And when the division is attended by relatives' (al-Nisa 8); and He said: 'And know that whatever you take as spoils, indeed for Allah is one-fifth of it and for the Messenger' (al-Anfal 41); for the fifth is known from the four-fifths only through division. And from the Sunnah: his (the Prophet's) direct practice of it in spoils and inheritances. And Ijma. (Completion from Fath). 'On segregation and exchange': meaning that division comprises the meaning of segregation, which is distinction, and the meaning of exchange; because there is no specified part except it comprises two shares, so what accumulates in one's portion, some of it was his and some was his partner's, so it becomes compensation for what is in his partner's hand. Thus it is exchange in that he takes his partner's right in exchange for his right, and segregation in that he also receives his right. This is according to us (Hanafis) and according to Malik: if the type and description are the same, it is segregation; otherwise it is exchange. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: it is segregation in all cases; and from al-Shafi'i: it is sale in all cases. (Miskeen and al-'Ayni). 'And it is the apparent' i.e., segregation is apparent in fungible goods (mithli) such as measured, weighed, and countable items that are similar; because there is no differentiation among their parts; for what each one takes from his partner's share is exactly like his own right in form and meaning, so it can be considered the very same his right. For this reason, it is considered the very same his right in qard (loans), sarf (currency exchange), and salam (forward sale). (Thus in al-Zayla'i and al-Fath).
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Division (Kitab al-Qismah) and provides a thorough definition and analysis of the concept. The author first connects division to preemption: preemption was legislated to repel harm to a neighbor, while division is legislated to complete the benefit of ownership; both involve compulsion (jabr) and relate to joint shares. The legal definition of division is 'a joint share in a specified [property]' — meaning that each co-owner has a share in every part, and division segregates those shares. The passage explains that division involves two elements: ifraz (segregation) and mubadala (exchange). Every part contains both owners' shares, so when one receives a part, he gets his own right (ifraz) and also the other's share in that part in exchange for what he gave up in the other's portion (mubadala). The text then distinguishes two types: division in physical objects (a'yan) and division in benefits (manafi'), though only the first is defined. The pillar (rukn) of division is any action that distinguishes shares, like measuring or weighing. Its cause (sabab) is the request of any partner; if no one requests, division is invalid. Its condition (shart) is that no benefit is lost in the process — the segregated portion must retain its original nature and benefits. Its ruling (hukm) is the identification of each share. Evidence is cited from the Quran (verses about water division, attending relatives, and spoils), the Sunnah (the Prophet's practice), and Ijma. The text then discusses the Hanafi position: division is both ifraz and mubadala. It compares with other schools: Malik agrees with the dual nature but limits ifraz to identical types; Shafi'i and Ahmad consider it always ifraz; some Shafi'is consider it a sale. The preferred view in the Hanafi school is that ifraz is dominant for fungible goods (mithliyyat) because there is no difference between portions, so it is like taking one's exact due.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): The legal process of dividing jointly owned property, involving segregation and possible exchange, to determine each owner's exclusive share.
إفراز (ifraz): Segregation; the act of separating each owner's share from the joint property, such that each takes exactly what is his own.
مبادلة (mubadala): Exchange; in division, this refers to the mutual transfer of shares between partners so that each gets full ownership of a specific part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is the division of fungible goods (mithliyyat) classified?
A) Pure segregation (ifraz) only
B) Pure sale (bay')
C) Both segregation and exchange, with exchange dominant
D) Neither, it is prohibited

Key Takeaway
Division (qismah) in Hanafi fiqh is not a single act; it combines segregation and exchange, with the dominant nature determined by whether the property is fungible or non-fungible.

The Qasim's Duties and the Use of Lots in Partition
Explaining the qasim's duties and rules for partition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the duties of the qasim, explain the Hanafi position on dividing multiple jointly owned properties, and state that drawing lots is permissible and recommended (not wajib) with Quranic proof.

Core Idea
In Hanafi partition, the qasim ensures equality; lots assign shares to calm hearts, not to create entitlement.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details the procedure for dividing jointly owned property (qismah). It covers the qasim's tasks—measuring, appraising, separating shares—and an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether multiple properties can be combined in one division. Crucially, it explains that drawing lots (qur'ah) is a recommended means to satisfy partners, not a form of gambling, as proven by Quranic examples. Understanding these rules shows how Hanafi fiqh balances equity, practicality, and the psychological well-being of co-owners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qasim's essential duties
List the actions the qasim must perform: depict the property, adjust, measure, appraise, separate each share with access paths and water rights, label shares, and write partners' names. Note that measurement of land and valuation of buildings are necessary for equality.
These steps ensure transparency and fairness.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on combining properties
Compare Abu Hanifa's position (each property divided separately) with the Sahibayn's view (can combine if beneficial). Identify the reasoning: location/neighbors vs. same type. Extend this to lands, and note the exception for houses in two cities.
The dispute rests on whether location differences are significant enough to prevent combination.
3
Understand the method and legal basis for drawing lots
Study the described method: determine smallest share, write names on slips, fold like pellets, embed in clay, draw. Note that the Imam may dispense with lots. Understand the Quranic proofs (Surah Al-Imran 44, Surah As-Saffat 141) and the distinction from gambling.
The lots only assign pre-existing rights, not create new ones.
4
Note exceptions and practical considerations
Review the rule about watercourses/paths: if a share has a path through another's land, it must be diverted if possible; otherwise the division is rescinded. Also note that dirhams cannot be forced into the division to balance shares without consent.
These exceptions maintain the integrity of separate ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة. مسائل القسمة: أي يسويه على سهام القسمة لأن المالية تعرف بالتقويم على حدة، ويصور القاسم ما يقسمه ويعدّله ويذرعه ويقوم البناء، ويفرز كل نصيب بطريقه وشربه ويلقب الأنصباء بالأول والثاني والثالث، ويكتب أسامي الشركاء. فإن كانت دور متباينة في محلة أو محلتين وطلبا من القاضي قسمتهما، فإنه يقسم كل دار على حدة، ولا يقسمها قسمة واحدة إلا بتراضيهما. وقالا: إن رأى القسمة أصلح يقسم بعضها في بعض؛ لأنها من جنس واحد، فصار كبيتين في دار واحدة. وله: أنها تتفاوت بالمكان والجيران تفاوتا فاحشا ومبناها على التساوي. وعلى هذا الخلاف الأراضي المشتركة. والدور في مصرين لا تقسم بالإجماع فيما رواه هلال، وعن محمد أنها تقسم. وأما الدار والضيعة أو الدار والحانوت، فلاختلاف الجنس فيتوهم فيه الربا. ويصور القاسم: أي يكتب على قرطاس؛ ليمكنه حفظه. قال في "العناية": يكتب أن نصيب فلان كذا، وفلان كذا إن أرادوا رفع تلك الكاغذة إلى القاضي؛ ليتولى الإقراع بينهم بنفسه. ويذرعه: لأن قدر الساحة يعرف بالذرع، والمالية بالتقويم، ولابد من معرفتها؛ ليمكنه التسوية في المالية، ولابد من ذرع الأرض وتقويم البناء. ويفرز كل نصيب أي يميز كل نصيب بطريقه وشربه؛ لأن القسمة لتكميل المنفعة، وبه يكمل؛ لأنه إذا لم يفرز يبقى نصيب بعضهم متعلقا بنصيب الآخر، فلم يحصل الانفصال من كل وجه، وهذا بيان الأفضل، فإذا لم يفرزه، أو لم يمكن جاز. قال في "الفتح" بعد ما نقل من "الزيلعي" وغيره: كون الإفراز أفضل، وفيه تأمل مع ما سيأتي من قولهم وإن قسم ولأحدهم مسيل أو طريق في ملك الآخر صرف عنه إن أمكن وإلا فسخت. واعلم أن في طريق الدور والأرض يكفي مرور رجل وثور ولا يشترط مرور الحمولة والعجل، كذا في القهستاني. ويلقب الأنصباء بالأول والثاني والثالث من طرف شاء، مثلا إذا جعل الجانب الغربي أولا يجعل ما يليه ثانيا، ثم ما يليه ثالثا إلى الآخر. ويكتب أسامي الشركاء ويقرع بينهم، فمن خرج اسمه أولا، فله السهم الأول، ومن خرج اسمه ثانيا، فله السهم الثاني، والقرعة لتطييب قلوبهم، فلو أقسم الإمام بلا قرعة جاز؛ لأنه في معنى القضاء. وكيفية القرعة: أن ينظر إلى أقل الأنصباء، فيقدر به آخر السهام حتى إذا كان العقار بين ثلاثة، لأحدهم النصف وللآخر الثلث وللآخر السدس، جعلها أسداسا؛ لأنه أقل الأنصباء، فيكتب أسماء الشركاء في بطاقات وتطوى كل بطاقة، ويجعلها شبه البندقة ويدخلها في طين، ثم يخرجها حتى إذا انشقت يدلكها، ثم يجعلها في كمه أو وعاء، فيخرجها واحدا بعد واحد فمن خرج اسمه أولا، فله السهم الأول، ومن خرج ثانيا، فله السهم الثاني، ومن خرج ثالثا، فله السهم الثالث إلى أن ينتهي إلى الأخير. فإن قلت: تعليق الاستحقاق بالقرعة قمار وهو حرام؟ قلت: لا نسلم؛ فإن الاستحقاق كان ثابتا قبلها وإنما صير إليها؛ لتطييب قلوبهم كالقرعة بين النساء للسفر أو للبداءة في القسم، وهذا ليس بقمار، وإنما القمار اسم لما يأخذون به شيئا لم يكن لهم قبل ذلك لا مثل هذه فإنها مشروعة كما أخبر الله تعالى حكاية عن يونس وزكريا عليهما السلام كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ يُلْقُونَ أَقْلَامَهُمْ أَيُّهُمْ يَكْفُلُ﴾ (آل عمران:44) الآية، وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَسَاهَمَ فَكَانَ مِنَ الْمُدْحَضِينَ﴾ (الصافات:141). ولا يدخل في القسمة الدراهم إلا برضائهم. فإن قسم ولأحدهم مسيل أو طريق في ملك الآخر صرف عنه إن أمكن.
Book of Partition. Issues of partition: meaning he makes it equal according to the shares of division because value is known by valuation separately. The divider depicts what he divides, adjusts it, measures it, appraises the building, separates each share with its path and watercourse, and labels the shares as first, second, third, and writes the names of the partners. If there are houses scattered in one or two localities and they request the judge to divide them, he divides each house separately, and does not divide them in one division except by their mutual consent. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he sees the division as more beneficial, he divides some in others because they are of one type, so it becomes like two rooms in one house. And for him (Abu Hanifa): They differ greatly by location and neighbors, and its basis is equality. Upon this difference is the case of shared lands. And houses in two cities are not divided by consensus according to what Hilal narrated, and from Muhammad that they are divided. As for a house and a farm, or a house and a shop, due to difference in type, riba is feared. And the divider depicts: meaning he writes on paper to be able to preserve it. He said in al-Inayah: He writes that the share of so-and-so is such, and so-and-so such, if they want to submit that paper to the judge to personally conduct the drawing of lots. And he measures it because the extent of the area is known by measurement, and value by valuation, and that must be known to enable equality in value, and measurement of land and appraisal of building are necessary. And he separates each share: meaning he distinguishes each share by its path and watercourse because division is to complete benefit, and it is completed by that; because if he does not separate, the share of some remains connected to the share of others, so separation from all aspects is not achieved, and this is a statement of the better, so if he does not separate it, or it is not possible, it is permissible. He said in al-Fath after quoting from al-Zayla'i and others: That separation is better, but there is reflection with what will come of their statement: And if he divides and one of them has a watercourse or path in the property of another, it is diverted if possible, otherwise the division is rescinded. And know that in the paths of houses and land, the passage of a man and an ox is sufficient, and the passage of a loaded camel and cart is not required, as in al-Qahistani. And he labels the shares as first, second, third from whichever side he wishes, for example if he makes the western side first, he makes what follows it second, then what follows it third to the end. And he writes the names of the partners and draws lots among them; whoever's name comes out first gets the first share, whoever's name comes out second gets the second share. The lot is to calm their hearts; if the imam divides without lots, it is permissible because it is in the meaning of qada. And the method of drawing lots: He looks at the smallest share and sets the last shares accordingly, so if the property is among three, one has half, another one-third, another one-sixth, he makes them sixths because it is the smallest share. He writes the partners' names on slips, folds each slip, makes it like a pellet, puts it in clay, then takes them out; when it splits, he rubs it, then puts them in his sleeve or a container, takes them out one by one; whoever's name comes out first gets the first share, second gets the second, third gets the third until the last. If it is said: Hanging entitlement on lots is gambling and it is haram? I say: We do not concede; because entitlement was established before it, and it is only resorted to to calm their hearts, like the lots among women for travel or for priority in division. This is not gambling; gambling is the name for taking something that one did not have before, unlike this, which is legislated as Allah informed in the story of Yunus and Zakariya, peace be upon them, as Allah said: {And when they cast their pens (to decide) which of them should be the guardian of Mary} (Al-Imran 44) and {Then he cast lots and was of the rebutted} (al-Saffat 141). And dirhams are not included in the division except with their consent. And if one of them has a watercourse or path in the property of another, it is diverted if possible.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of partition (qismah) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It opens by stating the duties of the qasim (divider): he must ensure equality based on value, which requires separate valuation of each share. He depicts the property by writing it down, adjusts, measures, and appraises the building. He then separates each share with its access path and water rights, and labels the shares as first, second, third, etc. The author then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: if there are multiple houses in different locations, Abu Hanifa holds that each house must be divided separately, because location and neighbors cause significant variation. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) say that if the qasim considers it more beneficial, he may combine them, as they are of the same type. This difference also applies to shared lands. For houses in two cities, Hilal reports consensus that they are not divided, but Muhammad divides them. For mixed types like a house and a farm, division is avoided due to fear of riba. The qasim's depiction on paper preserves the record for the judge if lots are drawn. Measurement of land and valuation of buildings are necessary for equality. Separation of shares into identifiable portions is recommended but not obligatory; if not done, the division is still valid. However, if a watercourse or path of one partner passes through another's allocated land, it should be diverted if possible; otherwise the division is rescinded. The minimum width for such paths is enough for a man and an ox. The qasim then labels shares, writes partners' names, and draws lots. The Prophet's practice shows drawing lots is not gambling; it is a means to satisfy the hearts, and the imam may even dispense with it. The method: determine the smallest fractional share (e.g., in a 1/2, 1/3, 1/6 situation, use sixths), write names on slips, fold them as pellets, embed in clay, then draw. The one whose name comes first gets the first share, and so on. This is supported by Quranic precedents. Finally, dirhams (money) cannot be forced into the division to balance shares unless all partners consent, because it would introduce a debt and risk injustice.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Partition of jointly owned property into distinct shares owned separately.
قاسم (qasim): The person appointed to divide the property, often an expert or judge.
قرعة (qur'ah): Drawing lots to assign shares among partners, used to calm hearts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on drawing lots (qur'ah) in partition?
A) It is wajib (obligatory).
B) It is sunnah (recommended) but not wajib.
C) It is haram (prohibited) because it resembles gambling.
D) It is makruh (disliked).

Key Takeaway
Partition in Hanafi fiqh is a precise process led by a qasim; drawing lots is a recommended means to assign shares without creating gambling.

Claim After Acknowledging Receipt of Share and Credibility of Partners
Ruling on a partner's claim after acknowledging receipt and on claims of taking part after division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on burden of proof when a partner claims after acknowledging receipt, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on hearing such a claim.

Core Idea
An acknowledgment of receipt is binding, but if error is shown, the claim is heard only with evidence.

This lesson examines two related scenarios in partnership division. In the first, a partner who acknowledged receipt later claims some of his share is with the other. In the second, a partner claims the other took part of his share. The Hanafi school establishes strict rules of evidence and oath to resolve such disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between the claim after acknowledgment and the claim of taking part (usurpation/mistake).
The first scenario involves a prior acknowledgment; the second does not rely on acknowledgment.
2
Determine the burden of proof
In the first scenario, the claimant is not believed without evidence because his acknowledgment contradicts his claim. In the second, the claimant also needs evidence, but the defendant is believed on oath if the claimant fails.
The key difference is the basis of the claim: error in division versus usurpation.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some early scholars said the claim after acknowledgment should not be heard at all due to contradiction. The accepted view is that it can be heard if the error becomes apparent.
This ikhtilaf is based on whether the acknowledgment is treated as absolute or contingent on the divider's accuracy.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Consider the examples: sheep division and immovable property. In each, the process of evidence and oath is the same.
The ruling is consistent across different types of property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ادعى أحد أن من نصيبه من الشركاء شيئا في يد صاحبه، وقد أقر بالاستيفاء، لم يصدق إلا ببينة، وإن قال: استوفيت أنا حقي وأخذت بعضه، صدّق خصمه بحلفه وإن لم يقر بالاستيفاء. [الشرح: ولو ادعى إلخ : أي إذا قسم العقار مثلا بين الشركاء، ثم ادعى أحدهم بعد أن كان أقر أنه استوفى حقه أن شيئا من نصيبه في يد الآخر، لم يصدق إلا ببينة؛ لأنه يدعي حق الفسخ لنفسه بعد تمامها، فلا يقبل إلا بحجة، فإن لم يقم البينة استحلف الشركاء؛ فمن حلف فقد برئ، ومن نكل جمع نصيبه مع نصيب المدعى عليه، فيقسم على قدر حقهما. واعلم أن بعض المشايخ قالوا: ينبغي أن لا تقبل دعواه أصلا؛ لأنه أقر بالاستيفاء أولا، وأنه عبارة عن قبض حقه كاملا، فإذا زعم أن مما أصابه شيئا في يد صاحبه، يكون متناقضا مبطلا للدعوى والشهادة، وفي "المبسوط" و"الخانية" ما يؤيد هذا، وفي "الذخيرة" : دعوى الغلط بعد سبق الإقرار باستيفاء الحق لا تسمع إلا من حيث الغصب، لكن وجه رواية المتن أنه اعتمد على فعل القاسم في إقراره باستيفاء حقه، ثم لما تأمل ظهر الغلط في فعله، فلا يؤاخذ بذلك الإقرار عند ظهور الحق. (عيني، ملا مسكين، فتح) صدق خصمه إلخ يعني إذا قال أحد الشريكين لآخر استوفيت أنا حقي ولكن أخذت أنت مني بعضه طولب بالبينة، فإن لم يقم كان القول قول خصمه مع يمينه؛ لأنه يدعي عليه الغصب، وهو ينكر، والقول قول المنكر، ولو اقتسما مائة شاة وقبضا، ثم ادعى أحدهما على صاحبه أنك أخذت خمسة من نصيبي غلطا ، وأنكر الآخر، وقال: اقتسمنا على أن يكون لي خمسة وخمسون، ولك خمسة وأربعون فالقول قوله مع يمينه؛ لأن القسمة قد تمت، ثم ادعى أحدهما على الآخر أنه أخذ خمسة غلطا وأنكر الآخر، فإن قامت بيئة عمل بها، وإلا استحلف المدعى عليه، كذا في "المحيط"، ففي المسألة الأولى هو يدعي الأخذ بطريق الغصب، وفي هذه الأخذ بطريق الغلط، فافترقا.
And if one claims that something of his share from the co-owners is in the hand of his partner, while he had acknowledged receipt, he is not believed except with evidence. And if he says: 'I received my right but you took some of it,' his opponent is believed with his oath, even if he did not acknowledge receipt. [Commentary: The statement 'and if one claims...' means: when immovable property is divided among partners, then one of them claims after having acknowledged that he received his right that something of his share is in the other's hand, he is not believed except with evidence; because he claims a right to rescind the division after its completion, so it is not accepted except with proof. If he does not produce evidence, the partners are asked to take an oath; whoever swears is cleared, and whoever refuses, his share is combined with the share of the defendant and divided according to their shares. Know that some mashaykh said: his claim should not be accepted at all, because he first acknowledged receipt, which is equivalent to taking his full right, so if he claims that something of his share is in his partner's hand, he is contradicting himself, invalidating the claim and testimony. In al-Mabsut and al-Khaniyyah, there is support for this. In al-Dhakhirah: a claim of error after prior acknowledgment of receipt of the right is not heard except as a claim of usurpation. But the explanation of the text is that he relied on the divider's action in his acknowledgment of receipt, and when he later reflects, the error in the divider's action becomes apparent, so he is not held to that acknowledgment when the truth appears. (Ayni, Mulla Miskin, Fath). The statement 'his opponent is believed etc.' means: when one of the two partners says to the other: 'I received my right but you took part of it from me,' he is required to produce evidence; if he does not, the word of his opponent with his oath is taken, because he claims usurpation against him, and he denies, and the word of the denier is accepted. And if they divided one hundred sheep and took possession, then one claims against the other: 'You took five of my share by mistake,' and the other denies and says: 'We divided on the basis that I get fifty-five and you get forty-five,' then the word of the latter with his oath is taken, because the division is complete. Then one claims against the other that he took five by mistake and the other denies; if evidence is produced, it is acted upon; otherwise the defendant is asked to take an oath. This is in al-Muhit. In the first case, he claims taking by way of usurpation; in this case, taking by way of mistake, so they differ.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage covers two scenarios. First, if a partner acknowledges that he received his full share but later claims some of it is still in the other partner's hand, he is not believed unless he brings evidence. This is because his acknowledgment is a form of admission that the division was complete, and to later claim otherwise is contradictory. Some scholars even say his claim should be rejected outright. However, the accepted view is that he can still claim if he shows that his acknowledgment was based on the divider's action and that a mistake has since become apparent. In the second scenario, if one partner says, 'I received my full share but you took part of it,' the other partner is believed on oath because he is the defendant denying a claim of usurpation. A similar rule applies if the claim is of a mistake in division, like in the example of dividing one hundred sheep, where the defendant's word is accepted on oath.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a fact, here specifically acknowledging receipt of one's share in division.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a partner acknowledges that he received his full share in a division, but later claims that some of his share is still in the other partner's hand, what is required for his claim to be heard?
A) He is automatically believed because of his previous acknowledgment.
B) He must produce evidence (bayyinah) to support his claim.
C) His claim is never accepted because he already acknowledged receipt.
D) He needs to take an oath that he is telling the truth.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, after a division, a partner's claim against another must be supported by evidence, and an acknowledgment of receipt strengthens the defendant's position.
