Sale of Human Excrement and Animal Dung: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on sale of impure manure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human excrement and animal dung, explain the Shafi'i objection, and apply the Hanafi response based on custom.

Core Idea
Custom determines property status: animal dung is saleable despite impurity.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the sale of two types of manure: human excrement (al-'adhrah) and animal dung (al-sirjin). The Hanafi school distinguishes between them based on customary usage, while the Shafi'i school prohibits both as impure substances that cannot be property. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi fiqh uses custom (urf) and tacit consensus (ijma suluti) to determine the validity of transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the rulings for human excrement (pure vs mixed) and animal dung as stated in the passage.
Selling pure human excrement is makruh tanzihi; when mixed with ash or earth, it is permissible. Animal dung is valid.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that the Shafi'i school prohibits selling both types because they are najis al-ayn (impure by essence).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i argument: Najasah prevents property status, and property is required for a valid sale.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: Custom (urf) shows that animal dung is treated as property; impurity does not negate property for solid non-liquid impure items.
For human excrement, pure is not customary but mixed is.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: Under Hanafi, both contracts are valid (with caveat for pure human excrement); under Shafi'i, the sale is invalid and money must be returned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كره بيع العذرة لا السرقين وهو الزبل والروث فصل في البيع إلخ آخر فصل البيع عن فصل الأكل والشرب واللمس والوطء؛ لأن أثر تلك الأفعال متصل يبدن الإنسان وما كان أكثر اتصالا كان أحق بالتقديم. (فتح) كره بيع إلخ أي كره بيع العذرة، وهي رجيع الآدمي؛ لأن العادة لم تجر بالانتفاع بها، وإنما ينتفع بها مخلوطة برماد أو تراب غالب عليها بالإلقاء الأرض، فحينئذ يجوز بيعها، والصحيح عن أبي حنيفة: أن الانتفاع بالعذرة الخالصة جائز، ونقل في 'الدر المختار' عن 'الملتقى': أن الانتفاع كالبيع أي في الحكم فافهم، قال في رد المحتار: الظاهر أنه أشار بنقله إلى أن تصحيح الانتفاع بالخالصة تصحيح الجواز بيعها أيضا، وقوله: 'فافهم' تنبيه على ذلك. واعلم أن الكراهة العذرة لا تقتضي البطلان، لكن يؤخذ من مقابلته بقوله: 'وصح مخلوطة' أن بيع الخالصة باطل، المذكورة في بيع كذا في 'رد المحتار على الدر المختار'. لا السرقين إلخ أي لا يكره بيع السرقين وهو الروث، بل يصح، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز؛ لأنه نجس العين، فلا يكون مالا، فلا يجوز بيعه كالعذرة، ولنا: أن المسلمين تمولوا السرقين من غير نكير، وهو ظاهر في أن المراد من كراهة بيع العذرة في كلام المصنف عدم جواز بيعها أي عدم صحته. (عيني (فتح
It is disliked to sell human excrement, not dung, which is dung and manure. Chapter on sale etc. ... (explanation) ... It is disliked to sell al-'adhrah, which is human feces, because custom does not involve benefiting from it, except mixed with ash or earth predominantly thrown on land; then its sale is permissible. The correct from Abu Hanifa is that benefiting from pure human excrement is permissible. ... Know that the dislike of human excrement does not imply invalidity, but from the contrasting statement 'and valid when mixed' it is understood that sale of pure is invalid. ... It is not disliked to sell al-sirjin, which is dung, rather it is valid. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible because it is impure by essence, so it is not property, so its sale is not permissible like human excrement. Our proof: Muslims have treated dung as property without any objection, and this shows that the intended meaning of dislike of selling human excrement in the author's words is non-permissibility i.e. its invalidity.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of impure substances, with an inter-madhhab dispute. The author states that selling human excrement (al-'adhrah) is disliked, but selling animal dung (al-sirjin) is not disliked. The commentator explains that the dislike applies to pure human excrement; when mixed with ash or earth, its sale becomes permissible. There is an intra-Hanafi discussion: Abu Hanifa considers benefiting from pure human excrement permissible, but some later scholars consider its sale invalid. The passage then contrasts the ruling on animal dung: it is valid to sell, unlike the Shafi'i school which prohibits it because it is impure by essence and thus not property. The Hanafi proof is that Muslims have customarily traded dung without objection, indicating its validity.

Key Terms
العذرة (al-'adhrah): Human excrement, which is impure but can be used as fertilizer when mixed.
السرقين (al-sirjin): Dung of animals, considered a commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi proof for the validity of selling animal dung?
A) The impurity of dung is removed by washing.
B) Muslims have customarily traded dung without objection (custom).
C) The Quran explicitly permits it.
D) Dung is not actually impure.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, custom (urf) can establish property status for impure items like animal dung, allowing their sale.

Buying a Slave-Girl from an Agent: Conditions for Validity
Conditions for purchasing from an agent claimant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a buyer may purchase a slave-girl from a person claiming to be an agent, and apply the Hanafi principle of accepting a single trustworthy person's statement in transactions.

Core Idea
A free, trustworthy seller's claim of agency is accepted; a slave's claim requires verification.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains when a buyer can purchase a slave-girl based solely on the seller's assertion of being an agent or owner. The ruling hinges on the seller's freedom and trustworthiness, and introduces the Hanafi concept of 'akbar al-ra'y' (dominant opinion) in evaluating truthfulness. We will unpack the conditions and their implications for everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the core situation: a buyer sees a seller claiming to be an agent for the owner of a slave-girl.
2
Examine the Seller's Status
Note whether the seller is free or a slave. If free, proceed; if a slave, further inquiry is needed.
A slave's word is not automatically accepted.
3
Assess Trustworthiness
The seller must be trustworthy (thiqah) or the buyer's dominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y) must lean toward truthfulness.
If the buyer's dominant opinion is that the seller is lying, he cannot purchase.
4
Apply to Transfer Claims
The same principle applies if the seller claims he bought it, was gifted it, or received it as charity.
5
Consider Possession and Knowledge
If the buyer does not know the original owner, possession (yad) is sufficient proof. If he knows, he must verify transfer.
Even a sinful possessor's word is accepted if ownership is unknown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له شراء أمة زيد قال بكر: وكلني زيد ببيعها أمة زيد وهذا لو كان البائع حرا، فلو عبدا أو أمة لم يحل الشراء قبل السؤال مطلقا. قال بكر وكلني إلخ أي من علم بأمة أنها لرجل فرأى آخر يبيعها حال كونه قال: وكلني صاحبها ببيعها، فإنه يسعه أن يشتريها ويطأها؛ لأنه أخبر بخبر لا منازع له فيه، وقول الواحد مقبول في المعاملات بشرط أن يكون مميزا على ما بينا من قبل هذا إذا كان البائع ثقة، أو كان أكبر رأيه أنه صادق فكذلك، وإن كان أكبر رأيه أنه كاذب لم يسعه يتعرض لشيء من ذلك. وكذا لو قال: اشتريتها منه أو وهبها لي أو تصدق بها عليّ، وإن لم يخبر بكر بشيء منها إن كان عرفها المشتري أنها لزيد لم يشترها حتى يعلم انتقالها إلى بكر، وإن لم يعرف وسعه اشترائها وإن كان ذو اليد فاسقا؛ لأن اليد دليل الملك، وإن كان الذي في يده عبدا أو أمة لم يشترها حتى يسأل، وإن أخبره أن مولاها أذن له، وهو ثقة، قبل قوله، وإلا يعتبر أكبر الرأي، وإن لم يكن له رأي لا يشتريها، ولو أخبر رجل امرأة أن زوجها الغائب مات أو طلقها ثلاثا، أو أتاها بكتاب منه بطلاق، ولا تدري أنه كتابه أم لا إلا أنه في أكبر رأيها أنه حق بعد ما تحرت، فلا بأس بأن تعتد ثم تتزوج، وكذا لو قالت لرجل طلقني زوجي وانقضت عدتي، فلا بأس بأن يتزوجها. (ملا مسكين، فتح عيني)
He may purchase the slave-girl of Zayd when Bakr says: Zayd appointed me to sell her. This applies if the seller is free; if the seller is a slave or slave-girl, it is not permissible to buy before asking at all. Bakr said 'he appointed me' means: whoever knows a slave-girl belongs to a man and sees another selling her while saying 'her owner appointed me to sell her', it is permissible for him to buy and have intercourse with her, because he reports something with no contestant; and the statement of a single person is accepted in transactions provided that person is discerning, as we explained before. This is when the seller is trustworthy, or his predominant opinion is that he is truthful. If his predominant opinion is that he is lying, it is not permissible for him to do anything. Similarly if he says 'I bought it from him' or 'he gifted it to me' or 'he gave it to me as charity'. And if Bakr did not inform him of anything, if the buyer knew that the slave-girl was Zayd's, he should not buy until he knows the transfer to Bakr. If he does not know, he may buy her even if the possessor is sinful, because possession is proof of ownership. But if the seller is a slave or slave-girl, he should not buy until he asks. If he informs him that his master permitted him, and he is trustworthy, his word is accepted; otherwise, consider the predominant opinion, and if he has no opinion, he may not buy. And if a man informs a woman that her absent husband died or divorced her three times, or he brings a letter from him with divorce, and she does not know if it is his letter except that her predominant opinion after investigation is that it is true, then it is fine for her to observe her waiting period and then marry. Similarly if she tells a man 'my husband divorced me and my waiting period ended', it is fine for him to marry her.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the permissibility of purchasing a slave-girl when the seller claims to be an agent or owner. The ruling distinguishes between free and enslaved sellers. If the seller is free and trustworthy, the buyer may accept his word and proceed with the purchase and even intimate relations. If the seller is a slave, the buyer must ask the owner or verify permission. The passage also extends similar principles to marriage cases where a woman is informed of her husband's death or divorce. The core ruling is that the statement of a single trustworthy person is accepted in transactions, based on the principle that possession is proof of ownership unless there is contrary evidence.

Key Terms
أكبر الرأي (akbar al-ra'y): Predominant opinion or strong assumption formed based on available evidence.
ثقة (thiqah): Trustworthy person whose statements are accepted in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can a buyer purchase a slave-girl from a seller who claims to be an agent?
A) The seller must be a free person and trustworthy
B) The buyer must have witnessed the agency contract
C) The seller must be a slave
D) The buyer must ask the owner directly

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mu'amalat, the statement of a single trustworthy person is accepted in transactions, provided the seller is free and the buyer's dominant opinion is in favor of truthfulness.

Ruling on Creditor Taking Price of Wine Sold by Debtor
Ruling on taking wine sale proceeds from Muslim vs. non-Muslim debtor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding a creditor taking the price of wine from a debtor and distinguish between a Muslim and non-Muslim debtor based on the concept of mal mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Wine is not valuable property for Muslims, so its sale is invalid; for non-Muslims it is valuable, so the price may be taken.

This lesson addresses a scenario where a creditor seeks repayment from a debtor who has sold wine. The ruling hinges on whether the debtor is Muslim or non-Muslim, based on whether wine is considered mal mutaqawwim (legally valuable property). We will also explore an intra-Hanafi difference when a Muslim debtor appoints a dhimmi to sell the wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The scenario: a creditor is owed a debt by a debtor. The debtor sells wine and uses the proceeds to pay off the debt. The creditor now possesses the price of wine. Is this permissible?
2
Determine the ruling for a Muslim debtor
If the debtor is Muslim, the sale of wine is invalid because wine is not mal mutaqawwim for Muslims. The debtor does not own the price; it remains the property of the buyer. Therefore, the creditor cannot take it.
The text states: 'لا يحل لرب الدين أن يأخذ ذلك' (it is not permissible for the creditor to take that).
3
Determine the ruling for a non-Muslim debtor
If the debtor is non-Muslim, the sale is valid because wine is mal mutaqawwim for them. The debtor owns the price, so the creditor may take it.
The text states: 'وإن كان البائع كافرا جاز له أن يأخذ' (if the seller is a non-Muslim, it is permissible for him to take).
4
Understand the legal rationale
The difference hinges on the concept of mal mutaqawwim. For a Muslim, wine is not valuable property that can be owned, so the sale is invalid (batil). For a non-Muslim, wine is considered valuable property, so the sale is valid and the price is owned.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the Shari'ah classification of property.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi exception
If a Muslim debtor appoints a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) to sell the wine on his behalf, Abu Hanifa holds that the creditor may take the price, as the sale is effectively the dhimmi's. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree.
This is a recognized exception within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره لرب الدين أخذ ثمن خمر باعها مسلم لا كافر المسلم أي وكره وكره لرب الدين إلخ يعني إذا كان لشخص مسلم دين على مسلم فباع الذي عليه الدين حمرا وأخذ ثمنها، وقضى الدين، لا يحل لرب الدين أن يأخذ ذلك بدينه، وإن كان البائع كافرا جاز له أن يأخذ، والفرق: أن البيع في الأول باطل، فلم يملك البائع الثمن، وهو باق على ملك المشتري، فلا يحل له أن يأخذ مال الغير بغير رضاه، والبيع في الوجه الثاني صحيح، فملك البائع الثمن؛ لأن الخمر مال متقوم في حق الكافر، فجاز له الأخذ، بخلاف المسلم. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٤/٨] وعلى هذا لو مات مسلم وترك ثمن حمر باعها هو، لا يحل لورثته أن يأخذوا ذلك؛ لأنه كالمغصوب، وعلى هذا قالوا: لو مات رجل وكسبه من بيع الباذق أو الظلم أو أخذ الرشوة تتورع الورثة، ولا يأخذوا منه شيئا، ويردونه على أربابه إن عرفوهم وإلا تصدقوا بها؛ لأن سبيل الكسب الخبيث التصدق أو الرد على صاحبه رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۳/۲] باعها مسلم إلخ إلا إذا وكل ذميا ببيعها طاب لرب الدين أخذه عند الإمام خلافا لهما، وقال عزي زاده: لابد من ذكر هذا الاستثناء، ولقائل أن يقول : لا حاجة إلى هذا الاستثناء؛ لأن البيع إذا أطلق ينصرف لما يكون بالمباشرة. (فتح)
And it is disliked for the creditor to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim, not a non-Muslim. 'Muslim' meaning it is disliked. 'For the creditor' means: if a Muslim person has a debt on a Muslim, and the debtor sells wine and takes its price and pays the debt, it is not permissible for the creditor to take that (the wine money) for his debt. If the seller is a non-Muslim, it is permissible for him to take. The difference: the sale in the first case is invalid, so the seller did not own the price; it remains the property of the buyer, so it is not permissible for him to take another's property without his consent. The sale in the second case is valid, so the seller owned the price because wine is valuable property for the non-Muslim, so it is permissible for him to take, unlike the Muslim. Exception: if he appointed a dhimmi to sell it, then it is permissible for the creditor to take according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage deals with a nuanced issue: a creditor seeking repayment from a debtor who has sold wine. The ruling distinguishes based on the debtor's religion. If the debtor is Muslim, the sale of wine is invalid, so the price he received is not actually owned by him; it still belongs to the buyer. Hence, the creditor cannot lawfully take that money. If the debtor is non-Muslim, wine is considered valuable property for them, so the sale is valid and the price belongs to the debtor; therefore, the creditor may take it. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: if a Muslim debtor appoints a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) to sell wine on his behalf, Abu Hanifa holds that the creditor may take the price, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree. Additionally, the ruling is extended to other unlawful earnings: inheritors should avoid taking wealth acquired through invalid means like selling wine or bribery; they should return it to the owners if known, otherwise give it as charity.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Valuable property that can be owned and traded legally in the Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it permissible for a creditor to take the price of wine from a non-Muslim debtor but not from a Muslim debtor?
A) Because non-Muslims are allowed to drink wine
B) Because wine is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for non-Muslims, so the sale is valid
C) Because Muslims cannot trade with non-Muslims
D) Because the non-Muslim debtor is trustworthy

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of taking the price of wine from a debtor depends on whether wine is considered mal mutaqawwim for that debtor, which differs between Muslim and non-Muslim.

Ruling on Hoarding Foodstuffs
Defines hoarding, its conditions, and exceptions with intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hoarding, identify the conditions that render it disliked, and explain the exceptions for one's own produce and goods from afar, citing the hadith and intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
Hoarding is disliked only when it harms the public; your own produce is exempt.

Hoarding (ihtikar) is a practice that has been discussed extensively in Hanafi fiqh. The following passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the scope of disliked hoarding, distinguishes it from permissible withholding of one's own property, and presents important intra-Hanafi differences between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this ruling helps us apply the principle that the public's right (haqq al-'amma) can restrict an owner's freedom to withhold goods.

Guided Path
1
Define hoarding and its basic ruling
From the passage, state what ihtikar is and whether it is haram, makruh, or permissible. Identify the default ruling and the conditions that trigger dislike.
The passage says it is disliked (makruh) when it harms the people.
2
Identify the proof (dalil)
Find the hadith cited and note how the Hanafi school derives karahah (dislike) rather than tahrim (prohibition) from it.
The hadith: 'al-jalib marzuq, wal-muhtakar mal'un'.
3
Analyze the exceptions
The passage exempts one's own produce (ghillah) and goods brought from another country. Explain the rationale: these are exclusive rights, not tied to public right.
Exceptions are based on the owner's full ownership.
4
Compare intra-Hanafi positions
Note Abu Yusuf's broader view (any harmful hoarding) and Muhammad's distinction based on the origin of goods. Understand the practical thamarah (difference).
This shows the range of opinion within the school.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a specific situation (e.g., a merchant withholds wheat in a small town), determine if it is disliked using the conditions from the passage.
Check for harm, size of town, duration, and source of goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتكار قوت الآدمي والبهيمة في بلد يضر بأهله لا غلة ضيعته وما جلبه من بلد آخر. كالشعير والتين أي أهل البلد أي لا يكره احتكار أي ولا احتكار كالبر ونحوه واحتكار إلخ يعني يكره الاحتكار في بلد يضر بأهلها؛ لقوله عليها: 'الجالب مرزوق، والمحتكر ملعون'، ولأنه تعلق به حق العامة وفي الامتناع عن البيع تضييق الأمر عليهم، فيكره إذا كان يضر بهم ذلك بأن كانت البلدة صغيرة، بخلاف ما إذا لم يضر بأن كان المصر كبيرا؛ لأنه حابس ملكه من غير إضرار لغيره، وتلقي الجلب على هذا التفصيل، وصورته أن يخرج من البلد إلى القافلة التي جاءت بالطعام واشترى منها خارج البلد وهو يريد حبسها، ويمتنع عن بيعها، ولم يترك حتى تدخل القافلة البلد قالوا: هذا إذا لم يلبس المتلقي سعر البلد على التجار، فإن لبس فهو محتكر في الوجهين وتخصيص الاحتكار بالأقوات قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف كل ما ضر بالعامة حبسه فهو احتكار وإن كانت دراهم ونحو ذلك، ثم الاحتكار إنما يكره إذا طالت المدة، وقدروها بأربعين يوما، وقيل بالشهر وقيل ضرب المدة للمعاقبة في الدنيا، وأما الإثم فحاصل وإن قصرت المدة، فالحاصل أن التجارة في الطعام غير محمودة إذا كان على قصد الاحتكار وتربص الغلاء، وقصد الإضرار بالناس، أما إذا لم يكن شيء من ذلك، فهو محمود. (عيني، ملا مسكين، تكملة) لا غلة ضيعته إلخ : يعني لا يكره احتكار غلة أرضه وما جلبه من بلد آخر؛ لأنه خالص حقه، فلم يتعلق به حق العامة، فلا يكون احتكارا، ألا ترى أن له أن لا يزرع ولا يجلب فكذا له أن لا . يبيع. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٥/٨] وعن أبي يوسف: يكره أن يحبس ما جلبه من بلد آخر؛ لإطلاق قوله عليه: 'المحتكر خاطئ'، وعن محمد: إن نقله من موضع يجلب منه إلى المصر في الغالب يكره حبسه، بخلاف ما إذا نقله من بلد بعيد لم تجر .
And hoarding the sustenance of humans and animals in a town that harms its people, not the produce of his land and what he brought from another town. Like barley and figs. 'Its people' meaning the people of the town, i.e., hoarding is not disliked. And hoarding etc. means: hoarding is disliked in a town that harms its people, due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'The one who brings goods is blessed, and the hoarder is cursed.' And because the right of the public is attached to it, and refraining from sale narrows matters for them. So it is disliked if it harms them, such as if the town is small, unlike if it does not harm, such as if the city is large, because he is merely withholding his property without harming others. ... And the specialization of hoarding to food is their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) saying. Abu Yusuf said: Everything whose withholding harms the public is hoarding, even if it is dirhams etc. Then hoarding is only disliked if it lasts long, and they estimated it as forty days, and it was said a month, and it was said that the specified period is for punishment in this world; as for sin, it occurs even if the period is short. ... 'Not the produce of his land etc.' means: hoarding the produce of his land and what he brought from another country is not disliked, because it is his exclusive right, so the public right is not attached, so it is not hoarding. Do you not see that he may choose not to cultivate or not to bring goods, so similarly he may choose not to sell. And from Abu Yusuf: it is disliked to withhold what he brought from another country due to the general implication of his saying: 'The hoarder is mistaken.' And from Muhammad: if he transfers from a place from which goods are normally brought to the city, hoarding it is disliked, unlike if he transfers from a distant country where it is not customary.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on hoarding (ihtikar). The author defines it as withholding food (for humans and animals) in a town where it harms the people. It is disliked (makruh) if the town is small and the hoarding causes harm. If the town is large and no harm is caused, it is not disliked. The proof is the hadith: 'The one who brings goods is blessed, the hoarder is cursed.' Exceptions are made for one's own produce (ghillah) and goods brought from another country, because these are exclusive rights of the owner and do not involve the public's right. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that hoarding anything that harms the public is disliked, not just food. Muhammad differentiates based on the origin of goods: if goods are normally brought from a particular place, withholding them is disliked; if from a faraway country, it is not. The passage also mentions the time period: hoarding is only disliked if it lasts a long time, estimated at forty days or a month. The sin occurs even for a short period if the intention is to harm.

Key Terms
احتكار (ihtikar): Withholding essential goods from sale to cause price increase; legally, the disliked hoarding of foodstuffs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is hoarding not disliked?
A) When it involves food for animals
B) When it involves goods from the hoarder's own produce
C) When the hoarder is a non-Muslim
D) When the hoarder sells at market price

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ihtikar (hoarding) is disliked only when it involves food, causes public harm, and persists for a long period; your own produce and goods from afar are exempt.

Creditor Taking Wine Money: Basic Ruling and Rationale
Basic ruling on creditor and wine money with rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a creditor accepting the price of wine sold by a Muslim debtor and explain why the ruling differs when the debtor is a non-Muslim.

Core Idea
Wine is non-valuable for Muslims, so its sale is invalid and the price is not owned by the debtor.

This passage presents a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning the creditor's acceptance of payment from a debtor who sold wine. The ruling hinges on the legal status of wine as property (mutaqawwim) and the validity of the sale. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi concept of property value and its implications for transaction validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
Identify the act: creditor taking the price of wine from a debtor. Note the debtor can be Muslim or non-Muslim.
2
State the ruling for each case
From the matn: it is disliked (kariha) for the creditor to take the price if the debtor is Muslim; permissible if the debtor is non-Muslim.
The commentary clarifies that 'disliked' here means not permissible (la yahillu).
3
Trace the rationale (ta'lil)
The commentary provides the reason: for a Muslim, wine is not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim), so the sale is invalid (batil). The debtor does not own the price; it remains the buyer's property. Taking it would be taking another's property without consent, which is haram. For a non-Muslim, wine is valuable property, so the sale is valid and the debtor owns the price.
4
Apply the ruling to the creditor's situation
Since the debtor does not legally own the wine money, the creditor cannot accept it as payment for the debt. The debt remains unpaid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره لرب الدين أخذ ثمن خمر باعها مسلم لا كافر. أي وكره لرب الدين إلخ يعني إذا كان لشخص مسلم دين على مسلم فباع الذي عليه الدين حمرا وأخذ ثمنها، وقضى الدين، لا يحل لرب الدين أن يأخذ ذلك بدينه، وإن كان البائع كافرا جاز له أن يأخذ، والفرق: أن البيع في الأول باطل، فلم يملك البائع الثمن، وهو باق على ملك المشتري، فلا يحل له أن يأخذ مال الغير بغير رضاه، والبيع في الوجه الثاني صحيح، فملك البائع الثمن؛ لأن الخمر مال متقوم في حق الكافر، فجاز له الأخذ، بخلاف المسلم. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٤/٨] وعلى هذا لو مات مسلم وترك ثمن حمر باعها هو، لا يحل لورثته أن يأخذوا ذلك؛ لأنه كالمغصوب، وعلى هذا قالوا: لو مات رجل وكسبه من بيع الباذق أو الظلم أو أخذ الرشوة تتورع الورثة، ولا يأخذوا منه شيئا، ويردونه على أربابه إن عرفوهم وإلا تصدقوا بها؛ لأن سبيل الكسب الخبيث التصدق أو الرد على صاحبه رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۳/۲] باعها مسلم إلخ إلا إذا وكل ذميا ببيعها طاب لرب الدين أخذه عند الإمام خلافا لهما، وقال عزي زاده: لابد من ذكر هذا الاستثناء، ولقائل أن يقول : لا حاجة إلى هذا الاستثناء؛ لأن البيع إذا أطلق ينصرف لما يكون بالمباشرة. (فتح)
And it is disliked for the creditor to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim, not a non-Muslim. That is, it is disliked for the creditor, meaning: if a Muslim owes a debt to a Muslim, and the debtor sells wine and takes its price and pays off the debt, it is not permissible for the creditor to take that as his debt. But if the seller was a non-Muslim, it is permissible for him to take it. The difference is that the sale in the first case is invalid, so the seller does not own the price, and it remains the property of the buyer, so it is not permissible for him to take another's property without his consent. In the second case, the sale is valid, so the seller owns the price, because wine is valuable property with respect to the non-Muslim, so it is permissible for him to take it, unlike the Muslim. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/404. And based on this, if a Muslim dies and leaves the price of wine he sold, it is not permissible for his heirs to take it, because it is like usurped property. And based on this, they said: if a man dies and his earnings are from selling fuqqa' (date liquor) or injustice or taking bribes, the heirs should avoid it and not take anything from it, and return it to its owners if they know them, otherwise give it in charity, because the way of evil earnings is charity or returning to its owner. Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/383. 'He sold it as a Muslim' etc. except if he appointed a dhimmi to sell it, then it becomes good for the creditor to take it according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions. And 'Izzi Zadah said: This exception must be mentioned, but someone may say: There is no need for this exception, because when the sale is absolute, it refers to what is done directly. (Fath).
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on a creditor accepting the price of wine from a debtor's sale. The matn says it is disliked (makruh) for the creditor to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim, but not if sold by a non-Muslim. The commentary clarifies that if the debtor is a Muslim who sold wine, the sale is invalid (batil) because wine is not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) for a Muslim. Therefore, the debtor does not own the price; it remains the property of the buyer. The creditor cannot take what is not the debtor's rightful property. However, if the debtor is a non-Muslim, wine is valuable property for him, so the sale is valid, and the debtor owns the price. The creditor may then take it. The commentary extends this: if a Muslim dies leaving wine money, it is like usurped property; heirs should not take it but return it or give it in charity. There is a difference of opinion regarding the case where the Muslim appoints a dhimmi as agent to sell the wine. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the creditor may then take the price, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree because the Muslim is still the principal. The commentary notes that some scholars consider this exception unnecessary.

Key Terms
رب الدين (rabb al-dayn): The creditor, the person to whom a debt is owed.
ثمن خمر (thaman khamr): The price received from selling wine, which is not lawful property for a Muslim.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Legally valuable property that can be owned and traded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a creditor accepting the price of wine sold by a Muslim debtor?
A) Permissible (halal)
B) Disliked (makruh) but not haram
C) Not permissible (haram)
D) Permissible only with the buyer's consent

Key Takeaway
A creditor cannot accept payment from a Muslim debtor from the sale of wine because the sale is invalid and the debtor does not own the price.

Appointing a Dhimmi to Sell Wine: Dispute Between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on creditor taking wine money when Muslim uses dhimmi agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference of opinion between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a Muslim debtor appointing a dhimmi agent to sell wine, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa permits taking wine money from a Muslim's dhimmi agent; the Sahibayn prohibit it.

The previous lesson established the basic ruling that a creditor cannot take wine money from a Muslim debtor. However, there is an important exception mentioned in the commentary: if the Muslim debtor appoints a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) to sell the wine on his behalf, Imam Abu Hanifa permits the creditor to take the price. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. This lesson examines the reasoning behind each view and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific scenario
A Muslim debtor owes a debt. Instead of selling the wine himself, he appoints a dhimmi as his agent (wakil) to sell the wine. The dhimmi sells it and the proceeds are used to pay the creditor. Does the ruling change?
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it is permissible for the creditor to accept the money. His reasoning: the sale is performed by the dhimmi, for whom wine is valuable property. The Muslim is not directly selling; the agent is the direct actor.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that it is still disliked (makruh) for the creditor to accept. Their reasoning: the Muslim is the principal and owner of the wine; the agent is merely an executor. The ruling should be based on the principal's legal capacity, not the agent's.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preferred view)
The commentary cites 'Izzi Zadah as saying the exception must be mentioned, implying that the Sahibayn's view is more cautious and perhaps preferred. Later scholars typically adopt the Sahibayn's position as mufta bihi.
5
Apply the practical difference
If the Muslim debtor appoints a dhimmi to sell wine, according to Abu Hanifa the creditor may take the money; according to the Sahibayn, the creditor should not take it. The mufta bihi is to not take it (Sahibayn's view).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره لرب الدين أخذ ثمن خمر باعها مسلم لا كافر. أي وكره لرب الدين إلخ يعني إذا كان لشخص مسلم دين على مسلم فباع الذي عليه الدين حمرا وأخذ ثمنها، وقضى الدين، لا يحل لرب الدين أن يأخذ ذلك بدينه، وإن كان البائع كافرا جاز له أن يأخذ، والفرق: أن البيع في الأول باطل، فلم يملك البائع الثمن، وهو باق على ملك المشتري، فلا يحل له أن يأخذ مال الغير بغير رضاه، والبيع في الوجه الثاني صحيح، فملك البائع الثمن؛ لأن الخمر مال متقوم في حق الكافر، فجاز له الأخذ، بخلاف المسلم. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٤/٨] وعلى هذا لو مات مسلم وترك ثمن حمر باعها هو، لا يحل لورثته أن يأخذوا ذلك؛ لأنه كالمغصوب، وعلى هذا قالوا: لو مات رجل وكسبه من بيع الباذق أو الظلم أو أخذ الرشوة تتورع الورثة، ولا يأخذوا منه شيئا، ويردونه على أربابه إن عرفوهم وإلا تصدقوا بها؛ لأن سبيل الكسب الخبيث التصدق أو الرد على صاحبه رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۳/۲] باعها مسلم إلخ إلا إذا وكل ذميا ببيعها طاب لرب الدين أخذه عند الإمام خلافا لهما، وقال عزي زاده: لابد من ذكر هذا الاستثناء، ولقائل أن يقول : لا حاجة إلى هذا الاستثناء؛ لأن البيع إذا أطلق ينصرف لما يكون بالمباشرة. (فتح)
And it is disliked for the creditor to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim, not a non-Muslim. That is, it is disliked for the creditor, meaning: if a Muslim owes a debt to a Muslim, and the debtor sells wine and takes its price and pays off the debt, it is not permissible for the creditor to take that as his debt. But if the seller was a non-Muslim, it is permissible for him to take it. The difference is that the sale in the first case is invalid, so the seller does not own the price, and it remains the property of the buyer, so it is not permissible for him to take another's property without his consent. In the second case, the sale is valid, so the seller owns the price, because wine is valuable property with respect to the non-Muslim, so it is permissible for him to take it, unlike the Muslim. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/404. And based on this, if a Muslim dies and leaves the price of wine he sold, it is not permissible for his heirs to take it, because it is like usurped property. And based on this, they said: if a man dies and his earnings are from selling fuqqa' (date liquor) or injustice or taking bribes, the heirs should avoid it and not take anything from it, and return it to its owners if they know them, otherwise give it in charity, because the way of evil earnings is charity or returning to its owner. Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/383. 'He sold it as a Muslim' etc. except if he appointed a dhimmi to sell it, then it becomes good for the creditor to take it according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions. And 'Izzi Zadah said: This exception must be mentioned, but someone may say: There is no need for this exception, because when the sale is absolute, it refers to what is done directly. (Fath).
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on a creditor accepting the price of wine from a debtor's sale. The matn says it is disliked (makruh) for the creditor to take the price of wine sold by a Muslim, but not if sold by a non-Muslim. The commentary clarifies that if the debtor is a Muslim who sold wine, the sale is invalid (batil) because wine is not valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) for a Muslim. Therefore, the debtor does not own the price; it remains the property of the buyer. The creditor cannot take what is not the debtor's rightful property. However, if the debtor is a non-Muslim, wine is valuable property for him, so the sale is valid, and the debtor owns the price. The creditor may then take it. The commentary extends this: if a Muslim dies leaving wine money, it is like usurped property; heirs should not take it but return it or give it in charity. There is a difference of opinion regarding the case where the Muslim appoints a dhimmi as agent to sell the wine. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the creditor may then take the price, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree because the Muslim is still the principal. The commentary notes that some scholars consider this exception unnecessary.

Key Terms
رب الدين (rabb al-dayn): The creditor, the person to whom a debt is owed.
ثمن خمر (thaman khamr): The price received from selling wine, which is not lawful property for a Muslim.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Legally valuable property that can be owned and traded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible (Abu Hanifa's view)
B) Disliked (Sahibayn's view)
C) Impermissible (haram)
D) Permissible only with the buyer's consent

Key Takeaway
When a Muslim appoints a dhimmi to sell wine, the mufta bihi is that the creditor should still not accept the price, following the Sahibayn's caution.

Hoarding Foodstuffs: Ruling, Conditions, and Exceptions
Ruling on hoarding with conditions and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hoarding, identify the conditions that turn it into a disliked act, and apply the exceptions for one's own produce and distant imports.

Core Idea
Hoarding is only disliked when it harms the public and is limited to essential foodstuffs.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains when hoarding (ihtikar) is considered legally objectionable. The matn states a general ruling with two key exceptions. The commentary unpacks the conditions and adds nuances from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Hoarding is disliked (makruh) when the conditions are met. The matn uses 'kariha' and the hadith mentions a curse, indicating it is close to haram.
The ruling is makruh tahrimi.
2
Locate the conditions for dislike
The dislike is conditional on: (1) goods being essential foodstuffs for humans and animals, (2) hoarding in a place where it harms the people (e.g., small town), and (3) the intention to withhold and cause harm.
In a large city where no harm occurs, it is not disliked.
3
Note the exceptions
Two exceptions are given: (1) the produce of one's own land, and (2) goods brought from another town. These are not considered hoarding because they are the owner's exclusive right with no public claim.
These exceptions are based on the principle that the owner may choose not to cultivate or import.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf extends hoarding to anything that harms the public, even money. Muhammad distinguishes goods that are customarily imported from those brought from distant places without custom.
The relied-upon position restricts hoarding to foodstuffs and upholds the exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتكار قوت الآدمي والبهيمة في بلد يضر بأهله لا غلة ضيعته وما جلبه من بلد آخر. أي وأيضا يكره الاحتكار في بلد يضر بأهلها؛ لقوله عليه: "الجالب مرزوق، والمحتكر ملعون"، ولأنه تعلق به حق العامة وفي الامتناع عن البيع تضييق الأمر عليهم، فيكره إذا كان يضر بهم ذلك بأن كانت البلدة صغيرة، بخلاف ما إذا لم يضر بأن كان المصر كبيرا؛ لأنه حابس ملكه من غير إضرار لغيره، وتلقي الجلب على هذا التفصيل، وصورته أن يخرج من البلد إلى القافلة التي جاءت بالطعام واشترى منها خارج البلد وهو يريد حبسها، ويمتنع عن بيعها، ولم يترك حتى تدخل القافلة البلد قالوا: هذا إذا لم يلبس المتلقي سعر البلد على التجار، فإن لبس فهو محتكر في الوجهين وتخصيص الاحتكار بالأقوات قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف كل ما ضر بالعامة حبسه فهو احتكار وإن كانت دراهم ونحو ذلك، ثم الاحتكار إنما يكره إذا طالت المدة، وقدروها بأربعين يوما، وقيل بالشهر وقيل ضرب المدة للمعاقبة في الدنيا، وأما الإثم فحاصل وإن قصرت المدة، فالحاصل أن التجارة في الطعام غير محمودة إذا كان على قصد الاحتكار وتربص الغلاء، وقصد الإضرار بالناس، أما إذا لم يكن شيء من ذلك، فهو محمود. (عيني، ملا مسكين، تكملة) لا غلة ضيعته إلخ : يعني لا يكره احتكار غلة أرضه وما جلبه من بلد آخر؛ لأنه خالص حقه، فلم يتعلق به حق العامة، فلا يكون احتكارا، ألا ترى أن له أن لا يزرع ولا يجلب فكذا له أن لا يبيع. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٥/٨] وعن أبي يوسف: يكره أن يحبس ما جلبه من بلد آخر؛ لإطلاق قوله عليه: "المحتكر خاطئ"، وعن محمد: إن نقله من موضع يجلب منه إلى المصر في الغالب يكره حبسه، بخلاف ما إذا نقله من بلد بعيد لم تجر العادة بالحمل منه إلى المصر في الغالب؛ لأنه لم يتعلق به حقهم [رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۳/۲]
And hoarding the food of humans and animals in a town where it harms its people, except the produce of his estate and what he has brought from another town. That is, hoarding is disliked in a town where it harms its people, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The importer is blessed, and the hoarder is cursed.' And because the right of the general public is attached to it, and refraining from selling causes hardship upon them. So it is disliked when it harms them, such as when the town is small, unlike when it does not harm, such as when the city is large, because he is withholding his property without harming others. And intercepting goods before they reach the market is subject to this detail: its form is that he goes out of the town to the caravan that has brought food and buys from them outside the town, intending to withhold it and refrain from selling, and does not leave until the caravan enters the town. They said this is when the interceptor does not confuse the market price for the merchants; if he confuses, he is a hoarder in both cases. And the specification of hoarding to foodstuffs is their statement. Abu Yusuf said: Everything whose withholding harms the general public is hoarding, even if it is dirhams and the like. Then hoarding is only disliked if the period is long, and they estimated it as forty days, and it is said a month, and it is said that the period is prescribed for punishment in this world; as for sin, it occurs even if the period is short. So the conclusion is that trading in food is not praiseworthy if it is with the intention of hoarding and waiting for high prices and intending to harm people; but if none of that is present, it is praiseworthy. (Ayni, Miskin, Takmila) 'Except the produce of his estate' etc.: meaning, hoarding the produce of his land and what he has brought from another town is not disliked, because it is his pure right, so the right of the general public is not attached to it, so it is not hoarding. Do you not see that he may choose not to cultivate or import, so likewise he may choose not to sell. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/405. And from Abu Yusuf: It is disliked to withhold what he has brought from another town, due to the general application of his saying (peace be upon him): 'The hoarder is mistaken.' And from Muhammad: If he has transported it from a place from which goods are usually brought to the city, it is disliked to withhold it, unlike if he has transported it from a distant town from which goods are not usually carried to the city, because the right of the people is not attached to it. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/383]
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on hoarding (al-ihtikar). The matn states that hoarding the food of humans and animals is disliked (makruh) in a town where it harms the people, with two exceptions: (1) the produce of one's own land, and (2) goods he has brought from another town. The commentary elaborates: hoarding is disliked because of the hadith 'the hoarder is cursed' and because it harms the public by restricting supply. The harm condition is met when the town is small; in a large city, withholding may not cause harm. The act of intercepting goods (talaqqi al-jalb) is a form of hoarding if done with the intent to withhold. There is a difference of opinion: Abu Yusuf extends hoarding to anything that harms the public, even money. The duration of hoarding matters; scholars estimated forty days as a threshold for sin, but sin occurs even if less. The exceptions are because one's own produce and goods from afar are his exclusive right, not tied to public need. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on whether one may withhold what he has brought: Abu Yusuf says it is still disliked if it harms; Muhammad distinguishes based on whether the goods are customarily imported.

Key Terms
احتكار (ihtikar): Withholding essential goods from sale to drive up prices, harming the public.
قوت الآدمي والبهيمة (qut al-adami wa al-bahimah): Foodstuffs that sustain humans and animals, e.g., wheat, barley, figs.
تلقي الجلب (talaqqi al-jalb): Going out to meet caravans before they reach the market to buy goods and hoard them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is it considered hoarding (ihtikar) if a farmer withholds the produce of his own land from sale?
A) Yes, because all withholding is hoarding.
B) No, because it is his exclusive right and no public right is attached.
C) Yes, if the town is small.
D) No, only if he brought it from another country.

Key Takeaway
Hoarding is only legally objectionable when it involves essential foodstuffs, harms the public, and is intentional; exceptions exist for personal produce and distant imports.

Price Fixing and Transactions That May Assist Sin
Ruling on price fixing and transactions that may assist sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule against price fixing in Hanafi fiqh, identify the exception when it is permissible, and compare the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions on transactions that might assist sin.

Core Idea
The ruler cannot fix prices; it is Allah's domain. But if sellers charge extortionately, the ruler may intervene by consultation.

This lesson examines two related sets of rulings from the matn. First, the general prohibition on price fixing by the ruler, based on a hadith that pricing is Allah's prerogative, with an exception for extreme exploitation. Second, several transactions—selling grape juice to a wine merchant, renting a house for non-Muslim worship, and hiring a non-Muslim to carry wine—where the permissibility depends on whether the sin is inherent in the object or caused by the user's free choice. Imam Abu Hanifa generally permits these, while his two companions consider them disliked. The later scholars followed the companions for renting and hiring, but the permissibility of selling grape juice remains the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general prohibition of tas'ir
Begin by recognizing that the ruler should not fix prices under normal circumstances. The hadith 'The one who fixes prices is Allah' establishes that pricing is a divine prerogative. The price is the seller's right, so the ruler should not interfere.
This is the default position. The ruler's intervention is only justified by extreme necessity.
2
Identify the exception and its conditions
Study the exception: if the sellers engage in 'fahish' (excessive) transgression, exceeding the fair value significantly, and the ruler cannot protect the people without fixing prices, then it becomes permissible after consulting experts. The condition is that the sellers refuse to sell except at an exorbitant profit (double the market value).
The exception is narrowly defined to prevent abuse of power.
3
Analyze the three transaction cases
For each transaction (selling grape juice, renting a house, hiring a carrier), note the base ruling: permissible (jaza) according to the matn, which represents Imam Abu Hanifa's view. Then note the companions' objection: they consider it makruh because it assists sin, citing the verse 'Do not cooperate in sin and transgression' and the hadith cursing those involved with wine.
The key distinction is whether the sin is inherent in the object/act or arises from the user's choice.
4
Trace the Hanafi ta'lil (legal reasoning)
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the contract (sale, rent, hire) is on a lawful subject matter (grape juice, house benefit, carrying service). The disobedience is not a necessary consequence; the other party has free choice to use it lawfully or unlawfully. This is analogous to selling a female slave to someone who might commit zina—the seller is not accountable for the buyer's sin.
The ta'lil distinguishes between direct assistance to sin (haram) and indirect transactions where sin is avoidable.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
For renting and hiring, the later three scholars (al-thalatha) adopted the companions' view of karaha (disliked), so the fatwa is that these are makruh. For selling grape juice, the permissibility remains the relied-upon view. For price fixing, the default is prohibition, with the exception as described.
The intrafi a ikhtilaf is resolved by tarjih: the companions' view is preferred for renting and hiring due to caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسعر السلطان إلا أن يتعدى أرباب الطعام عن القيمة تعديا فاحشا، وجاز بيع العصير من حمار وإجارة بيت ليتخذ فيه بيت نار أو بيعة أو كنيسة، أو يباع فيه خمر بالسواد، وحمل الخمر لذمي بأجر. أي لا ينبغي للسلطان والإمام أن يسعر على الناس مطلقا لا في الغلاء ولا في الرخص؛ لقوله عليه: "المسعر القابض الباسط الرازق"؛ ولأن الثمن حق البائع وكان إليه تقديره، فلا ينبغي للإمام أن يتعرض لحقه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٥/٨] إلا أن يتعدى إلخ: أي لا يسعر السلطان إلا إذا تعدى أرباب الطعام بالتحكيم على المسلمين تعديا فاحشا وعجز السلطان عن صيانة حقهم إلا بالتسعير فلا بأس به حينئذ بمشورة أهل الرأي والنظر، ولا يسعر إلا إذا أبوا أن يبيعوه إلا بغين فاحش ضعف القيمة رمز الحقائق: ٣٨٣/٢] وجاز وكره عند بعض العلماء. (ملا) بيع العصير إلخ لأن المعصية لا تقوم بعينه بل بعد تغيره، بخلاف بيع من أهل الفتنة؛ لأن المعصية تقوم بعينه، فيكون إعانة لهم وتسببا، وقد نهينا عن التعاون على العدوان والمعصية، ولأن العصير يصلح للأشياء كلها جائزة شرعا، فيكون الفساد إلى اختياره تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٦/٨] وإجارة بيت يعني جاز إجارة البيت لكافر ليتخذه معبدا أو بيت نار للمجوس، أو يباع فيه حمر في السواد، وهذا قول الإمام، وقالا : يكره كل ذلك؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَتَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْبِرِّ وَالتَّقْوَى وَلَا تَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْإِثْمِ وَالْعُدْوَانِ﴾ (المائدة: ۲)، وبقولهما قالت الثلاثة وله أن الإجارة على منفعة البيت ولهذا تحب الأجرة بمجرد التسليم، ولا معصية فيه، وإنما المعصية بفعل المستأجر، وهو مختار، فقطع نسبة ذلك إلى المؤجر، وصار كبيع الجارية لمن لا يستبرئها أو يأتيها في دبرها، أو بيع الغلام ممن يلوط به تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٠٧/٨] والدليل عليه لو آجره للسكنى جاز ولابد فيه من عبادته. قال في "الفتح": لكن في "حاشية الشلبي" بالعزو إلى الولوالجي: يكره بيع الأمرد ممن يعصي فيه؛ لأنه إعانة على المعصية، وفيها عن "المحيط": اشترى المسلم الفاسق عبدا أمرد وكان ممن يعتاد اتباع الأمرد يجبر على بيعه. (عيني، فتح) بالسواد: أي القرى دون الأمصار، وهو قيد للأربعة الأخيرة، فيجوز بيع العصير من الخمار مطلقا، وإن لم يكن بالسواد، وإليه يشير كلام الزيلعي ولا ينافيه قول العيني: وهذا قيد للكل؛ لأنه يراد بالكل ما عدا الأول، وإنما قيده بالسواد؛ لأنهم لا يمكنون ذلك في الأمصار؛ لظهور شعائر الإسلام بخلاف السواد وقالوا هذا في سواد الكوفة؛ لأن غالب أهلها من أهل الذمة، وأما في سواد غيرها، ففيه شعائر الإسلام ظاهرة، فلا يمكنون فيها في الأصح. (تكملة، فتح , عيني) وحمل الخمر إلخ يعني جاز ذلك، ويطيب له أجره عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا يكره؛ لأنه عليه لعن في الخمر عشرة، وعد منها حاملها، وله أن الإجارة على الحمل، وهو ليس بمعصية، ولا بسبب لها، وإنما تحصل المعصية بفعل فاعل مختار، وليس الشرب من ضرورات الحمل؛ لان حملها قد يكون للإراقة والتخليل، فصار كمن استأجره =
And the ruler should not fix prices unless the food sellers exceed the value in an excessive transgression. And it is permissible to sell grape juice to a wine merchant, and renting a house to be used as a fire temple, a church, a synagogue, or to sell wine in the countryside, and hiring a non-Muslim to carry wine for a wage. That is, it is not proper for the ruler or imam to fix prices for people absolutely, neither in times of high prices nor low, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The one who fixes prices is the One who constricts, expands, and provides.' And because the price is the right of the seller and its estimation belongs to him, so it is not proper for the imam to encroach upon his right. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/405. 'Unless they exceed' etc.: That is, the ruler should not fix prices unless the food sellers exceed by overcharging the Muslims in an excessive transgression and the ruler is unable to protect their right except by price fixing, then there is no harm at that time with the consultation of people of opinion and insight. He should not fix prices unless they refuse to sell except at an excessive profit, double the value. Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/383. 'And it is permissible' (some scholars disliked it). (Mulla) Selling grape juice etc., because the disobedience is not inherent in it but after its transformation, unlike selling to people of fitnah, because the disobedience is inherent in it, so it is assistance to them and a cause, and we are forbidden from cooperating in sin and transgression. And because grape juice is suitable for all things that are legally permissible, so the corruption is left to choice. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/406. 'And renting a house' means it is permissible to rent a house to a non-Muslim to make it a place of worship, a fire temple for Zoroastrians, or to sell wine in the countryside. This is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, but the two companions said: all of that is disliked, due to the verse: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety, and do not cooperate in sin and transgression' (5:2). And by their saying the three (later scholars) gave fatwa. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the contract of rent is on the benefit of the house, thus the rent is due upon mere delivery, and there is no disobedience in that; the disobedience is only by the action of the tenant, who is free to choose, so that action is not attributed to the lessor. This is like selling a female slave to someone who may not observe istibra or commit sodomy, or selling a beardless youth to someone who may commit sodomy with him. Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/407. The proof for it is that if he rents it for dwelling, it is permissible, and inevitably there will be some worship in it. In al-Fath it says: But in Hashiyat al-Shalabi, with reference to al-Walwaliji, it is disliked to sell a beardless youth to someone who will commit sin with him, because it is assistance to sin. And in it from al-Muhit: If a sinful Muslim buys a beardless slave and is accustomed to following beardless youths, he is forced to sell him. (Ayni, Fath) 'In the countryside' means the villages rather than the cities, and this is a condition for the last four (rulings). So it is permissible to sell grape juice to a wine merchant absolutely, even if not in the countryside, and this is indicated by the statement of al-Zaylai, and it does not contradict the statement of al-Ayni that this is a condition for all, because by 'all' he means other than the first. It was restricted to the countryside because they would not allow that in the cities due to the manifestation of Islamic symbols, unlike the countryside. They said this applies to the countryside of Kufa because most of its inhabitants are dhimmis; as for the countryside of other places, Islamic symbols are apparent there, so they would not be allowed in the correct view. (Takmila, Fath, Ayni) 'And hiring a non-Muslim to carry wine' etc. means that is permissible, and his wage is lawful according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but the two companions said it is disliked, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) cursed ten in connection with wine, including the carrier. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the contract of hire is for carrying, which is not a sin, nor a cause of it; the sin only occurs by the voluntary action of the drinker. Drinking is not a necessary consequence of carrying, because it may be carried for pouring out or turning to vinegar. So it is like one who hires a man to do something.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings, all introduced by the matn. First, the ruler should not fix prices (tas'ir) based on the hadith that pricing is Allah's prerogative. However, if sellers charge exorbitantly, the ruler may fix prices by consultation. Second, it is permissible to sell grape juice (al-aseer) to a wine merchant because the sin is not inherent in the juice itself, unlike selling weapons to rebels. Some scholars dislike it. Third, renting a house to a non-Muslim for use as a place of worship or for selling wine is permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the contract is on the benefit of the house which is lawful; the sin is on the tenant. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it disliked because it assists sin. The later scholars (al-thalatha) gave fatwa with the companions. Fourth, hiring a non-Muslim to carry wine for a wage is permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, disliked by the companions, due to the hadith cursing the carrier. Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the act of carrying (lawful) and the subsequent drinking (not necessarily caused by the carry). The commentary also notes that selling a beardless youth to a known sinner is disliked. The term 'bil-sawad' (in the countryside) is a condition for the permissibility of these rulings except the first; in cities, it is not allowed because Islamic symbols are prominent.

Key Terms
تسعير (tas'ir): Price fixing by the ruler or authority, setting a maximum price for goods.
عصير (aseer): Grape juice that has not yet turned into wine; it is permissible to drink and sell.
سواد (sawad): Rural areas, villages, as opposed to cities (amsar).
إجارة (ijara): Contract of hire or rent; a lease for a specified benefit in exchange for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, under what condition may the ruler fix prices?
A) Whenever prices are high, to protect consumers.
B) Only when sellers exceed the value by an excessive margin (fahish) and the ruler cannot protect the people otherwise, after consultation.
C) Only when the sellers sell at a loss.
D) The ruler may never fix prices under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits transactions where the object or service is lawful, even if it may be misused, as long as the sin is not a necessary consequence of the contract; the ruler's price fixing is prohibited except in cases of extreme exploitation.

Price Control by the Sultan: When Is It Permissible?
Conditions for the sultan to impose price controls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling on price control and identify the exceptional condition that permits it, citing the supporting hadith.

Core Idea
Default: no price control; exception: when sellers excessively overcharge and public interest demands it.

In the Hanafi school, market freedom is the norm, but the state may intervene in extreme cases to prevent exploitation. This lesson examines the conditions under which the sultan may legally fix prices, balancing prophetic guidance with public necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Understand that the default position is that the sultan should not set prices. This is derived from the hadith prohibiting tas'ir.
The rule is rooted in trust in market forces and divine providence.
2
Recognize the exceptional condition
The condition for permissibility is when sellers exceed the fair price excessively (fahish) and the sultan cannot protect the people except by setting a price.
Excessive overcharging is the key trigger; minor fluctuations do not justify intervention.
3
Examine the proof
The hadith 'Do not set prices...' is the primary textual evidence. Analyze its implications and why it can be overridden by necessity (istihsan).
The hadith is ahad and zanni, allowing for exceptions based on stronger maslaha.
4
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario of gross overcharging, determine if price control becomes permissible, and note the requirement of consultation with knowledgeable people.
Consultation ensures the price is just and not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسعر السلطان إلا أن يتعدى أرباب الطعام عن القيمة تعديا فاحشا، ووجاز بيع ... يسعر ... "لا تسعروا فإن الله هو المسعر القابض الباسط الرازق"
And the sultan does not set prices except that the owners of food exceed the value excessively, and it becomes permissible to sell... he sets prices... "Do not set prices, for Allah is the One Who sets prices, the Constrictor, the Expander, the Provider."
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on price control (tas'ir) by the sultan. The default position is that the sultan should not fix prices on commodities, as indicated by the hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) states that Allah alone is the real price-setter. However, if the sellers engage in grossly unfair pricing that harms the public and the sultan cannot protect the rights of the people except by imposing a price cap, then it becomes permissible to do so, after consulting with knowledgeable and just people.

Key Terms
تسعير (tas'ir): The act of the sultan fixing a maximum price for goods
تعد فاحش (ta'addin fahish): Grossly excessive overcharging beyond the fair market value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible for the sultan to impose price controls?
A) Whenever he deems it fit to regulate the market
B) Only when sellers are charging a price that is more than double the market rate
C) When sellers are grossly overcharging and the sultan cannot protect the people otherwise, after consultation
D) Never, because it is prohibited absolutely

Key Takeaway
Price control is prohibited in principle but becomes permissible when sellers grossly overcharge and public necessity demands it, subject to consultation.

Selling Grape Juice to a Wine-Maker: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on selling grape juice to a wine-maker

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a sale of a permissible commodity is valid even if the buyer intends to use it for sin, and distinguish it from cases where the sin is inherent in the commodity itself.

Core Idea
Sin does not inhere in the grape juice; it arises only after the buyer's act of fermentation.

This lesson examines the ruling on selling grape juice to a wine-maker. The Hanafi school holds that such a sale is permissible because the juice itself is halal; the sinful act of making wine is a separate choice by the buyer. This ruling rests on a key distinction: sin must be inherent in the commodity itself for the sale to be prohibited, as in the case of selling weapons to rebels.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commodity and its inherent legal status
Recognize that grape juice is a permissible, pure commodity. It does not become wine until after fermentation.
The commodity's inherent nature is crucial.
2
Examine the buyer's intention and its effect
The buyer's sinful intention does not transfer sin to the commodity because the sin occurs only after the juice changes form.
The seller is not responsible for the buyer's future actions.
3
Contrast with the prohibited analogy
Compare with selling weapons to rebels, where the weapon is used directly for sin; here sin is inherent.
The illa (effective cause) is whether sin inheres in the commodity itself.
4
Extract the illa from the text
The text states 'sin does not inhere in its essence but only after its transformation' vs. 'sin inheres in its essence' for weapons.
The illa is 'qiyam al-ma'siyah bi 'ayni al-commodity'.
5
Apply to similar scenarios
Use the rule: if the commodity is halal and sin is not inherent, the sale is permissible regardless of buyer intent.
Examples: selling sugar to a brewer, selling dates to a winemaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجاز بيع العصير من خمار ... لأن المعصية لا تقوم بعينه بل بعد تغيره، بخلاف بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة؛ لأن المعصية تقوم بعينه
And it is permissible to sell grape juice to a wine-maker... because sin does not inhere in its essence but only after its transformation, unlike selling weapons to people of sedition; because sin inheres in its essence.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of selling grape juice ('asir) to a person who will use it to make wine (khamr). The Hanafi position is that such a sale is permissible because the grape juice itself is a lawful commodity. The sin of wine-making only occurs after the juice ferments and changes into an intoxicant. Thus, the seller is not directly assisting in sin. This is contrasted with selling weapons to rebels or people engaged in fitnah, where the weapon is used immediately for sin, making the sale impermissible.

Key Terms
العصير (al-'asir): Grape juice, a permissible drink before fermentation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on selling grape juice to someone who will make wine?
A) Permissible, because sin does not inhere in the juice itself
B) Impermissible, because it facilitates sin
C) Only permissible if the buyer is a non-Muslim
D) Only permissible if the seller is a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
A sale of a permissible commodity is valid even if the buyer intends to use it for sin, as long as the sin is not inherent in the commodity itself.

Selling Real Estate in Mecca: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on selling real estate in Mecca with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi ruling on selling real estate in Mecca and explain the evidence from hadith and custom.

Core Idea
Selling land in Mecca is permissible; the Prophet's acknowledgment of 'Aqil's homes proves ownership and transferability.

This passage addresses a debated issue within the Hanafi school: whether land and buildings in Mecca can be sold. While some early narrations suggest dislike, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is permissibility, supported by prophetic hadith and continuous Muslim practice. Understanding this ruling clarifies the school's application of scriptural evidence and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under discussion
The passage discusses the permissibility of selling buildings (buyut) and land (arad) in Mecca. This is a specific category of real estate transactions.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) and one narration from Abu Hanifa, it is permissible (ja'iz). This is the mufta bihi.
3
Examine the evidence
The hadith "هل ترك لنا عقيل من رباع؟" (Has 'Aqil left us any homes?) acknowledges that 'Aqil owned properties in Mecca, implying ownership and transferability. Additionally, continuous practice (ta'āruf) from the time of the Prophet without objection is cited as strong evidence.
4
Recognize the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
There is another narration from Abu Hanifa that it is disliked (makruh), but this view is considered weak. The tarjih is based on the strength of the hadith and custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجاز بيع بناء بيوت مكة وأرضها... يعني جاز له ذلك؛ لأنه ملك لمن بناه، ولأنه لو بنى في المستأجر أو في الوقف صار البناء ملكا له، ( له بيعه. وأرضها: [أي بيع أرضها] أي أرض بيوت مكة، وهذا عندهما، [وبه يفتى] وهو إحدى الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة؛ لأن أراضيها مملوكة لأهلها؛ لظهور التصرف والاختصاص، ولقوله عليه: "هل ترك لنا عقيل من رباع؟" الحديث فيه دليل على أن أراضيها تملك، وتقبل الانتقال من ملك إلى ملك، وقد تعارف الناس ذلك من أول الإسلام إلى الآن من غير نكير، وهو من أقوى الحجج، وبه قال الشافعي، وبه يفتى
And it is permissible to sell the building of houses in Mecca and their land... meaning it is permissible for him because he owns what he built, and if he built on leased land or waqf, the building becomes his property, so it is permissible for him to sell it. And their land: i.e., selling their land, the land of the houses of Mecca, and this is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and this is the fatwa, and it is one of two narrations from Abu Hanifa; because their lands are owned by their people due to evident disposition and specialization, and due to his statement (peace be upon him): "Has 'Aqil left us any homes?" The hadith indicates that their lands are owned and can transfer from one ownership to another, and people have customarily done so from the beginning of Islam until now without objection, and this is the strongest evidence, and al-Shafi‘i said the same, and this is the relied-upon fatwa.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage resolves the dispute about selling property in Mecca. The majority view in the Hanafi school (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and one narration from Abu Hanifa) is that selling land and buildings in Mecca is permissible. The evidence includes the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned the homes left by 'Aqil, indicating ownership. The continuous practice of Muslims buying and selling property in Mecca from the earliest times also supports this. The other narration (reported as dislike) is considered weak. Thus, the fatwa is on permissibility.

Key Terms
رباع (riba' (plural of rab')): Homes or residential properties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi ruling on selling land in Mecca?
A) Permissible, as per the majority of Hanafi imams
B) Disliked, as per one narration from Abu Hanifa
C) Forbidden, based on the sanctity of Mecca
D) Only allowed for non-Muslims

Key Takeaway
Selling real estate in Mecca is permissible in the Hanafi school, supported by the Prophet's hadith about 'Aqil's homes and continuous Muslim practice.

Ruling on Selling Buildings and Land in Mecca
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the sale of Meccan land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the evidence for permissibility and prohibition of selling Meccan land, and identify the mufta bihi Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Meccan land is owned and can be sold — the mufta bihi Hanafi position relies on a strong hadith and continuous practice.

In this lesson, we explore a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of land in Mecca. While the sale of buildings themselves is straightforwardly permissible, the status of the underlying land has been debated. We will examine the proofs for both permissibility and prohibition, and understand why the Hanafi school has adopted the view that such land is owned and transferable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
The issue is whether land in Mecca can be sold. There are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one declaring it permissible (ja'iz), the other disliked (makruh). The mufta bihi position is permissibility, also held by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
The permissibility view is also the position of al-Shafi'i.
2
Examine the proof for permissibility
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Did 'Aqil leave us any dwellings?' This indicates that dwellings were owned and could be transferred. Additionally, continuous practice (ta'amul) from the time of the Prophet to the present day without objection is cited as a strong evidence.
This hadith is used to refute the claim that Meccan land is inalienable.
3
Examine the proof for prohibition
The opposing view relies on another hadith: 'Indeed Mecca is sacred, its dwellings may not be sold or inherited.' It also argues that the sanctuary (Haram) is a waqf of Prophet Abraham, and that the land was originally common property (sawa'ib).
The Hanafi school interprets this hadith as describing the early practice before individual ownership was established.
4
Understand the tarjih reasoning
The Hanafi scholars prefer the permissibility view for two reasons: (1) the strength of the proof from hadith and continuous practice, and (2) the practical necessity, as the land has been owned and transacted for centuries without objection.
The author states 'به يفتى' (with it fatwa is given), indicating the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجاز بيع بناء إلخ: يعني جاز له ذلك؛ لأنه ملك لمن بناه، ولأنه لو بنى في المستأجر أو فى الوقف صار البناء ملكا له، ( له بيعه. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٨٤/٢] وأرضها: [أي بيع أرضها] أي أرض بيوت مكة، وهذا عندهما، [وبه يفتى] وهو إحدى الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة؛ لأن أراضيها مملوكة لأهلها؛ لظهور التصرف والاختصاص، ولقوله عليه: "هل ترك لنا عقيل من رباع؟" الحديث فيه دليل على أن أراضيها تملك، وتقبل الانتقال من ملك إلى ملك، وقد تعارف الناس ذلك من أول الإسلام إلى الآن من غير نكير، وهو من أقوى الحجج، وبه قال الشافعي، وبه يفتى، وجه رواية الكراهة قوله عليه: "ألا إن مكة حرام لا تباع ،رباعها، ولا تورث؛ لأن الحرم وقف الخليل عليه، ولأن الأراضي بمكة كانت في زمن النبي عليه والخليفتين من بعده تدعى بالسوائب من احتاج إليها سكنها ، ومن استغنى عنها تركها. (تكملة)
And it is permissible to sell buildings etc.: meaning it is permissible for him because the building belongs to the one who built it, and because if he built in a leased property or in a waqf, the building becomes his property, so he may sell it. And her land: i.e., selling her land, i.e., the land of the houses of Mecca, and this is according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and with it fatwa is given, and it is one of two narrations from Abu Hanifa; because its lands are owned by their inhabitants due to apparent disposal and specialisation, and due to his statement: "Did 'Aqil leave us any dwellings?" The hadith contains evidence that its lands are owned and can transfer from ownership to ownership, and people have become accustomed to that from the beginning of Islam until now without objection, and it is among the strongest proofs, and al-Shafi'i agreed, and with it fatwa is given. The basis of the disliked narration is his statement: "Indeed Mecca is sacred, its dwellings may not be sold or inherited, because the sanctuary is a waqf of al-Khalil (Abraham), and because the lands in Mecca were in the time of the Prophet and the two caliphs after him called as-sawa'ib: whoever needed it inhabited it, and whoever had no need left it."
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling buildings and land in Mecca. The author explains that it is permissible to sell buildings because they are owned by the builder, even if built on leased or waqf land. Regarding the land itself, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is owned by its inhabitants and can be sold, as held by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and this is one of two narrations from Abu Hanifa. The proof includes the hadith 'Did 'Aqil leave us any dwellings?' indicating ownership and transferability, along with continuous practice since early Islam. The opposite view (karaha) is based on a hadith stating that Mecca's dwellings cannot be sold or inherited, considering the Haram as a waqf of Ibrahim. The commentary favors the permissibility view, noting that this is also the position of al-Shafi'i and the one given fatwa.

Key Terms
السوائب (as-sawa'ib): Common or public property; land that anyone could use freely without ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, what is the ruling on selling land in Mecca?
A) It is forbidden because the Haram is a waqf.
B) It is permissible and the land is owned by its inhabitants.
C) It is disliked (makruh) because the Prophet prohibited it.
D) It is only permissible for emergency needs.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position allows selling Meccan land based on strong hadith and continuous practice, contrary to the view of some early scholars who considered it common property.
